```
"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:jBwOk.292$Dm5.179@newsfe01.iad...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
<snip>>
> So COBOL-wise you are promoting small methods, correct ?
>

As I rarely write COBOL these days, I'm not "promoting" anything COBOL 
related.

I do believe that objects and their behaviours in ANY language should be 
small wherever possible.

> >>Did you for some $12 get a second-hand copy
> >>of Arranga/Coyle from amazon - most of their methods are no more than a
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Am I wrong, or am I wrong ? :-

You are wrong on two counts:

1. The sentence above has the qualifier MOST at the start of it. It doesn't 
say this is ALWAYS the case. There are exceptions (this is one of them 
because this Method is generalised code and has to deal with a number of 
different possibilities. Originally each of the sections was a separate 
method but, using the primitive SQL available in COBOL, I could find no way 
to share a cursor across different methods, so it had to be handled in ONE 
method. However, each of the functions within that method is in a section 
and the sections are small.

2. I never signed a contract with Arranga, Coyle or you that said I would 
abide by what you consider to be good practice.

I said I agreed with the principle and GENERALLY applied it, having come to 
the same conclusion many years ago.

But most importantly, why would I apply your black and white judgements to 
something that requires subtlety and consideration?

The code requires more than one section so it got more than one section. Get 
over it.

> >  IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
> >  METHOD-ID. HandleDB AS "HandleDB".
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> *** Nearly missed that - can you use W/S in Methods with Fujitsu ?.

Does it matter?

As there is a WS section coded and it has entries in it, and given that the 
code works, I should have thought the answer to your question is axiomatic.



>
> >  01  saved-SQLSTATE   pic x(5).
…[79 more quoted lines elided]…
> print ?

As I stopped printing program code some years ago, I have no idea.

Each of the sections is small and I already explained why the Method NEEDS 
to accommodate all the possible actions.

The code works. What's your problem exactly?

I do note in passing that you made no comment on what the code was 
demonstrating, so obviously your priority is to try and make an issue about 
something that is not an issue.

>>
>> Summing up:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Factory'.

Oh, I can't wait. Be still my beating heart...

> Hopefully I've included sufficient comment in the code to guide the 
> uninitiated readers, just a little.

I have no intention of "shooting" at anybody. It strikes me as sad that you 
see the need to.
<snipped>

Pete.
```

```
Alain Reymond wrote:
>>   
> The .NET direction is a very interesting way. Is there any experience of
> using programs developped with .NET Cobol and running under Linux with
> Mono ? Can it be made ? Maybe you know a little bit on that topic.

My understanding is that running Micro Focus .NET COBOL programs under
Mono currently isn't supported. I think there was some early
investigation into that which revealed some issues, and it hasn't been
pursued yet because there hasn't been much customer demand.

Personally, I think it would be a nice additional feature, but of
course until there's significant demand, we can't commit many
resources to it. That wouldn't be fair to customers who are asking for
things they need...

I may have a look at it myself, though, when I have a spare moment.
And certainly any Micro Focus customer, or prospective customer, who
would be interested in this should let someone at MF know.
```

```
Alain Reymond wrote:
>>   
> Well, it is always the problem between offer and demand. If it can run
> under Mono, won't it attract some customers ?!

I have no idea, to be honest. I haven't seen any market studies.

Market demand is certainly one factor in determining our development
priorities. (There are others, of course, such as maintenance
commitments to existing customers.) We have people who put a lot of
effort into gauging it; I'm not one of them.

That said, I'll remember that you asked about Mono support. The topic
comes up periodically, and "someone was asking for it" is definitely a
stronger argument than "it seems like it might be neat".

Also, please note that I don't speak for Micro Focus; these are my
personal opinions.
```

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-09T03:09:02+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6nlkrvFm7iqaU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<3e0efcba-4ea9-47a9-b362-084ba97255a4@v53g2000hsa.googlegroups.com> <FzdMk.631048$J72.602924@fe05.news.easynews.com> <983a0a41-c540-48a8-8b7d-6006ebf47822@p39g2000hsb.googlegroups.com> <pY5Rk.3$2l5.1@newsfe01.iad>`

```


"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:pY5Rk.3$2l5.1@newsfe01.iad...
> Nondisclosure007 wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> Jimmy

It's a very well produced/written paper and well worth the read.

Looking at the COBOL implementations of things I do every day in C# with VS 
2008, I'm not sorry I made the transition. COBOL is clumsy by comparison.

BUT, if you buy this compiler and IDE it DOES give you managed code in 
COBOL. For some people that would be an important consideration.

There is a tentativeness in the writing. The author knows that .NET is about 
Classes and Objects, but he/she also knows that COBOLWorld is generally not 
hip to these things and there will be resistance. As a result we get a very 
oversimplified summary of OO COBOL, and then detailed examples that probably 
will terrify the people reading them.

Nevertheless, it is a good implementation, and, if you intend to cling to 
COBOL til your last dying breath, it has to be an option.

In the final analysis, whether you like it or not, if you move to .NET you 
will be utilizing an Object Oriented, component based environment. This 
paper is saying that using indexed files and procedural code is viable in 
this environment.

I disagree.

You CAN do it (I'm not arguing about it being technically feasible...), but 
you won't enjoy it.

It is one thing to say: "I can write Windows Forms or even WPF applications 
in COBOL..."

It is quite another to match your application against another one running in 
the same environment that utilises full OO and is fully tiered with layers 
of components that interact and are reusable.

So, you gradually start getting into OO COBOL. With some perseverance and a 
bit of practise you can do (almost) anything that the other languages can 
do.  Good stuff. As time goes by, you get more facile with it, grasp more 
concepts and feel more comfortable. MF have made it possible for you, with 
this very good implementation.

There are two ways you can view this scenario:

1. Clinging to COBOL was a good thing and made your transition into 21st 
century programming much less painful and abrupt. It brought you time to 
modernise existing applications and was not traumatic on your programming 
staff. However, you have made no real progress. Yes, you have a better user 
interface which may extend the life of your apps a bit longer, but you paid 
dearly for it.

2. You have spent a lot of money on a product that you are eventually, and 
inevitably going to discard.

If you are responsible for a medium size business that is used to spending 
serious money for development tools, viewpoint 1 is probably no big deal.

On the other hand if you are a small business where money does not come 
easily and there is no room for unnecessary expenditure, you probably won't 
want to spend money on something that could be acquired for free.

Sooner or later your applications will have to be migrated anyway, and 
moving to .NET is a fantastic opportunity to get OFF flat files into DB 
objects, and to refactor and encapsulate the essential parts of your 
existing applications in a way that lets them co-exist with modern 
languages, while your programmers pick up the deal on Objects.

If you take option 1 and very gradually ease yourself into the new 
environment, you will reach a point where OO COBOL is "doing it" for you and 
you feel quite comfortable with this approach. As your experience with it 
grows you start to understand the advantages of it. Pretty soon there is no 
way you would go back to procedural coding.

Then you realise that other languages do it better and quicker. COBOL just 
takes too long and is still tied into maintaining source code, and grounded 
in procedural approaches.

As you are now sold on OO and components, you decide to let go, and move to 
C# (or VB.NET or C++.NET).

Should you have grasped the nettle and made this move at the point where you 
first considered moving to .NET?

(You would have saved thousands of dollars, some of which might have been 
used for training in Object Oriented programming...)

Or, was moving to .NET COBOL the right choice?

(OK, it cost, but the change gradient was flattened and the resulting trauma 
was lessened. Your people got to learn about OO COBOL in the topmost OO 
environment.)

Obviously, it is a subjective decision that cannot be generalised.

The bottom line is that you don't NEED a .NET compiler (MF OR Fujitsu) to 
run your COBOL on .NET, if you are OK with it not being managed code. We 
showed with the COBOL structure tool that standard COBOL (once it is 
converted to an OO COBOL component) can run perfectly well in the .NET 
environment, as unmanaged code, using InterOP Services.

I have a number of applications that are doing this. They integrate very 
well with C# and it allows me to leverage Legacy COBOL for no charge and 
without remaining locked in to it.

I really like the MF .NET version of COBOL, but I still think it is an 
"unnecessary" product.

Much of the productivity gain is provided by VS 2008 which I can download 
for free from MicroSoft.

C# lets me do more, faster, than COBOL ever did. It can be downloaded for 
free from MicroSoft.

One man's opinion. :-)

Pete.
```

```


"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:qNsOk.69$Zp6.42@newsfe01.iad...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> "Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> Smart Alec, but just a gut feeling.

Web programming, like all programming, is difficult until you master it.

But that's no reason not to attempt it.

Good luck with your endeavour.

What I was looking for was someone with a Micro Focus compiler who could 
simply take Robert's original code and convert it to a COM server using MF, 
just as I did using Fujitsu. There's no need for web wrapping or anything 
else really. It would then be possible to compare how Fujitsu deals with COM 
and how MF does it.  It took me a couple of hours, but it didn't work after 
being converted. Robert took it back and used his original code to figure 
out that initializations were failing. It was because each of the previously 
nested programs were now methods. Nested programs maintain their state 
between calls; object methods do not. They re-initialize each time they are 
invoked.We moved the fields that were getting initialized when they 
shouldn't into Object storage (common or global) and everything worked.
>
> The nearest I got was reading Will's SECOND BOOK which described HTML, 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> three months away, and of course it wont be dotNet.

It would be if you used ASP.NET. If you're starting from scratch you might 
as well be hung for a sheep as for a lamb... :-) You don't HAVE to use C# 
code-behinds and Master Pages. You could use JavaScript instead.

Whatever you decide will be a rich (and ultimately satisfying) learning 
experience.

I can recommend W3C Schools... I keep them bookmarked and refer to them for 
syntax sometimes. Here's a link:
http://www.w3schools.com/

Pete.
```

```
> 
> I'd still like someone with MF access to comment on support for COM (not 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> environment.

I have done very little with COM, personally, other than occasionally
using one of the Windows administration COM components from the odd
WSH script.

Here's what I know about our COM support in native-mode COBOL:

- There are a bunch of COM demos included with the product, including
standalone clients and servers, ActiveX controls, etc. The ones I
looked at use OO COBOL syntax, and seem pretty clean.

- Net Express includes Class and Method Wizards for creating COM
clients and servers.

- Procedural COBOL programs can be COM clients as well, just by adding
a class-control paragraph with the COM class ProgID or CLSID, an
object-reference data item, and a couple of invoke statements.

- The Interface Mapping Toolkit supports mapping COBOL programs as COM
servers. The IMTK is a pretty nice way to wrap existing COBOL code as
a service; it finds entry points and parameters, and lets you define
the methods and messages that map to them.

- There's a whole bookshelf on COM programming in the Net Express
documentation, including a chapter on using COM with .NET.

For .NET COBOL, it appears COM support is pretty straightforward. For
a .NET COM server, you define your interface and implementation; for a
.NET COM client, you add a reference to your Visual Studio project,
invoke the constructor to get a proxy reference, and invoke proxy
methods to send messages to the server.

> Fujitsu support for COM is excellent and has been for years now.

I haven't looked at the Fujitsu support, and I haven't really used
ours, so I can't say how they compare.

> As the imminent release of C# (version 4) is improving COM interaction, it 
> looks like COM is still relevant, at least in the MicroSoft arena.

Yes, I don't see it going away. Microsoft still use COM extensively.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol for Visual sutdio

```
"Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
news:geptf9187r@news1.newsguy.com...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Always a good way to get started.

Fujitsu achieves it by templates in PowerCOBOL and added support in the IDE 
for NetCOBOL.


> - Procedural COBOL programs can be COM clients as well, just by adding
> a class-control paragraph with the COM class ProgID or CLSID, an
> object-reference data item, and a couple of invoke statements.

Yes, it is pretty easy to wrap existing COBOL as COM.  However, It is better 
if the COBOL you are wrapping is already OO COBOL, in my opinion.

>
> - The Interface Mapping Toolkit supports mapping COBOL programs as COM
> servers. The IMTK is a pretty nice way to wrap existing COBOL code as
> a service; it finds entry points and parameters, and lets you define
> the methods and messages that map to them.

Sounds good...
>
> - There's a whole bookshelf on COM programming in the Net Express
> documentation, including a chapter on using COM with .NET.

It works through .NET's InterOP services and runs as unmanaged code. I was 
amazed at how few problems manifested themselves when I first tried doing 
this. (there were some, but they  were fairly easily resolved with some help 
from the MicroSoft community). I did sweat some of the interfacing with 
COBOL, but once I got under the covers and realised how it works I was able 
to use different compiler options which fixed it.  Nowadays I run COM 
components on .NET with no problems at all. All of the Data Access Layer 
objects generated by my Migration Toolset are COM components.

>
> For .NET COBOL, it appears COM support is pretty straightforward. For
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> methods to send messages to the server.

Yes, that is exactly how I do it. You need a reference to InterOP services 
if your COM server is not written in the .NET environment (Native code as 
opposed to CLR)

Like most things, after you do it a couple of times it becomes pretty easy. 
:-)

>
>> Fujitsu support for COM is excellent and has been for years now.
>
> I haven't looked at the Fujitsu support, and I haven't really used
> ours, so I can't say how they compare.

From what you have described it looks like both products provide good 
support for COM.
>
>> As the imminent release of C# (version 4) is improving COM interaction, 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Rhetoric & Writing, Michigan State University

Thanks very much for this Michael.

I'm glad Micro Focus have full COM support and the use of a Wizard is a very 
good way for people to wrap code and get into COM.

At some stage (when mycurrent workload has been completed) I'd REALLY like 
to see what is involved in using COM with Micro Focus and at a detail level 
what the support Classes can do.

My Migration Toolset currently supports Fujitsu COBOL but I'd very much like 
to provide the same tools for a Micro Focus environment.

I see COM/ActiveX as being very useful for the following reasons:

1. Platform independent data typing.
2. Full OO support of Methods Properties and Events, running on the desktop 
or the Web or both, with a standard interface.
3. Supported in both MicroSoft and LAMP environments. (Can be brokered 
through CORBA)
4. Proper and secure support (now... this wasn't true in early 
implementations) for remote procedure calls across the network.
5. COM components are completely language independent and can be 
called/invoked by any language that supports calling. They are isolated from 
languages and environments, and that means you can mix them into any 
environment you choose to use, now and in the foreseeable future.
This has HUGE implications for migrating COBOL apps.

I have been very impressed with the ability to put functionality anywhere it 
is needed, and share it efficiently across a network if required.

I'm glad C# (4) is beefing up its already good support for it.

Pete.
```

```

Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:d0354600-0c9f-4979-ac64-84127e96412b@q26g2000prq.googlegroups.com...
> And checking the formatted listing and the flowchart and the execution
> path is done how ?  Visually ???  Laboriously ?? Error prone ? Do you
> think that merely printing a flowchart proves the program to be
> correct.

That is a rather superficial reply, don't you think?  There is a vast
difference between reading a program listing and reading a formatted summary
of same.

>
>
> In fact the 'obvious checks' you refer to may well be obvious to you,
> the question is: will the next programmer know that those checks are
> 'obvious' and do them ?

The obvious checks I referred to are those involved in testing.  Less
precise than I should have been.

>
>
> > Many times I have had to maintain or make
> > changes to programs written in styles that I didn't like, but by
conforming
> > to that style I almost always did the job properly and learned something
in
> > the process.  I STILL don't like goto-less programming but I'm not about
to
> > say that it shouldn't be used for that reason.
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I didn't say I didn't use it.  I did say I don't like it.  Your statement
"Cobol went beyond goto-less programming 20 years ago" is too generalized.
The controversy started in the '70's, as I recall, and has never been
settled in a decisive sense - i.e,  a step-by-step logical argument which
leads to an inescapable conclusion.  It HAS been proved that you can write
perfectly well without using GOTO's - well, almost, because there was a
lengthy discussion in this group some time ago about a verb to break out of
loops - which was all about replacing a GOTO with something that did the
same function but wasn't called a GOTO.  And the chosen construct lacked the
unerring explicitness of the GOTO.

It is not my intention to start another style war, especially over a subject
which will never be settled until everyone is dead - or perhaps, as PD
posits, there comes a time when programming as we inderstand it will no
longer exist.  Style wars generally - i.e., most of the time - boil down to
"I do it this way and everyone else should too".  I have argued with people
who won't use COMPUTE for stylistic reasons of their own!  Way back when -
in the '70's when I was with Univac - one of our customers upgraded to a
9480 from a 9300 (for those who weren't there - a 9300 was a 32k machine
with disk & tape; a 9480 was 256k with disks & tapes and multiprogramming.
The customer would not use multiprogramming for love or money because he
didn't trust it - he was always afraid that the programs would interfere
with each other, corrupt each other's data, if they ran simultaneously.  He
was soon replaced, because the 9480 cost considerably more than the 9300,
and the only advantage he got was that it used a faster printer; that would
have been OK but the machine was also replaced, because the new guy was a
Honeywell groupie.  Both of them had a "style" - a way of doing things -
which weren't necessarily common.  Anyway, the point is that people will
stake out their own undertanding and practice, and defend it with might and
main if attacked over it.  Most people will change, albeit slowly and
secretly, if given time to retreat with dignity - IF the change is worth
making.  If a programming style works well, and is readily maintainable (and
I don't plan to get involved in defining either of those, so don't bother
poking me), then nothing more needs to be said.  When it comes time for the
program to be replaced, rewritten, or enhanced significantly - then styles
may become important.

PL
```

```
On Nov 2, 7:57 am, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
> Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> That is a rather superficial reply, don't you think?

No, I don't. You claimed that a visual check of the code is too
laborious and error prone. I am saying that there are styles where the
check that would be done by visual inspection of a flowchart is simply
not necessary. Without gotos there cannot be a goto to the wrong
place. Without exit paragraphs there cannot be a wrongly named or
placed exits. Without section headers there cannot be a missing
section terminator.

> There is a vast
> difference between reading a program listing and reading a formatted summary
> of same.

That may depend on how the program has been written and laid out. In a
structured program each paragraph is a 'formatted summary' of the
lower level paragraphs.

In any case a summary is not all the code, so bugs in the deatil may
be hidden.

> > In fact the 'obvious checks' you refer to may well be obvious to you,
> > the question is: will the next programmer know that those checks are
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

And what if it not 'obvious' that particular items need to be tested
for ?


> > > Many times I have had to maintain or make
> > > changes to programs written in styles that I didn't like, but by
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> leads to an inescapable conclusion.

Or perhaps it has actually been 'settled', except that you simply
closed your mind and ignored that.

The point about goto is that there is nothing at all wrong with a goto
statement. It is perfectly clear what will happen and there is no
ambiguity. What is wrong with _using_ goto is that there must be a
label that is the target. When visually checking the goto there is no
issue. When visually checking somewhere else and there is a label then
_that_ is where the problem lies.

The only way to resolve all possible logic paths though or around that
label is to examine the whole program to see if there is a drop
through, goto , perform or thru. It also needs checking to see if it
should actually have a section keyword that would affect the code
above.

Now some styles, such as the one under discussion, use signals to make
the checking easier. For example the use of an exit paragraph signals
that the next label _must_ have a section keyword. But it is still
only a visual check, the compiler won't complain.

You apparently think that it is perfectly natural and reasonable to
have to make these checks, it is just part of writing programs.


> It HAS been proved that you can write
> perfectly well without using GOTO's - well, almost, because there was a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> unerring explicitness of the GOTO.

I don't use EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION/PERFORM but they do have the
advantage over goto that you can't specify the wrong paragraph label.
They do have significant disadvantages in that they are positional, as
is goto. The code that I write can be moved around because I do not
use anything that depends on its position. So I can take the content
of an inline perform and move it to a separate paragraph, replacing it
with a perform. If that did contain a goto exit or exit p/s/p then the
program would stop working correctly. Or, worse, it may work well
enough to get through the testing.

The ending of perform loops are done by the UNTIL.

You may have it as an 'obvious check' that when moving code between in-
line and out-of-line you need to check the gotos, exits, and whatever
else and add that condition to the testing. I write code that never
needs that check.

Or perhaps you never move code between in-line and out-of-line, or
never use in-line because those 'obvious checks' are too laborious and
error prone.


>
> It is not my intention to start another style war, especially over a subject
> which will never be settled until everyone is dead - or perhaps,

I suspect that may be the way it will be. Some will never change their
mind regardless of the arguments or advantages.

> as PD
> posits, there comes a time when programming as we inderstand it will no
> longer exist.

It seems that it will always exist in your cubicle.

> Style wars generally - i.e., most of the time - boil down to
> "I do it this way and everyone else should too".  I have argued with people
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> may become important.

So, you are saying that you are like the first manager of that
customer and when you are replaced because you are still using the old
slow methods then your code will be thrown out and some other language
moved in ???
```

```


"Richard" <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:f164ac18-d2ab-48e8-88e4-098083d068c0@c22g2000prc.googlegroups.com...
> On Nov 2, 7:57 am, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
>> Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> section terminator.

I had more or less decided not to re-engage in this for the following 
reasons:

1. I don't entirely disagree with you, although I think you are being a bit 
bloody minded about things that should be picked up by peer review of code, 
test results and compliance with installation standards.
2. I don't use COBOL very much any more (certainly not for my own stuff)
3. There's no way I'm going to change the way I do things in COBOL now. 
(However, I certainly wouldn't advocate it is the ONLY way and everyone else 
should emulate it.

What has prompted me to post is your paragraph above...

There is no argument that if GO TOs are eliminated then things cannot GO .. 
TO the wrong place. They may still arrive there by other mechanisms but 
certainly GO...TO won't be a culprit.

Without EXIT paragraphs there won't be WRONG exits and, I agree that with 
all the EXIT variants in COBOL we probably don't need them.

I don't know what you mean by a "section terminator"... for me that is 
simply the next section header. I usually stick a row of hyphens or 
something in there to make it more physically apparent.

I think the problem here Richard, is that you are working on the assumption 
that a COBOL program can contain all kinds of mixed styles. You are right. 
Of course it can.

But my argument is predicated on a SPECIFIC style, laid down and enforced by 
system standards with peer review to ensure compliance.

The style I use (and I re-iterate, I am not advocating it is the ONLY good 
one, or people should comply with it...) does NOT allow a mix of  performed 
paragraphs and sections. The entire program is composed of sections and 
there are no performs of paragraphs. EXIT paragraphs can only mark the end 
of a section under these circumstances. (Putting one in the middle of a 
section would be stupid, and banned by the standard, as there can only BE 
ONE exit per section.) However, I take your point that they are redundant 
with modern code constructs. Nevertheless, I'll continue using them for 
habit and old time's sake :-) (remember I don't write much COBOL these 
days...if I did, I might reconsider.)

Also I think I'd rather see a section with a series of GO TO MyExit 
statements in it than a series of EXIT SECTION statements. It's sort of like 
an expectation that the section must exit through the exit and EXIT SECTION 
means you are left "hanging" with regard to this expectation... :-)

(I'm not totally serious about that; I feel quite sure that once I started 
using EXIT SECTION I'd become just as comfortable with it as I am with 
GO..TO exit. (Which I use extremely rarely, BTW...)

The bottom line is that no minds are likely to be changed because, as you 
are well aware, we all like what we are used to. Unless it was seriously 
problematic (and in my experience I have not seen my style cause anybody 
problems) then it is unlikely to be changed.

I am very certain that your code does not cause problems, but neither does 
mine. The possible problems you have identified are all theoretically 
posible, but not if certain constraints are added to the environment. (God 
knows, I hate adding constraints to anything, but if an installation has 
standards then I have no problem coding the way they want.)

I'd finish this by saying I have debugged other people's code as well as my 
own and really, the style is irrelevant. If you're a programmer, that's what 
you do. Much has been made here of the 2:00am guy trying to understand what 
something does; I've never been persuaded by that argument, even when I WAS 
the 2:00am guy... If a site thinks it is OK to let someone with no knowledge 
of the application loose on it at 2 in the morning, then I reckon they 
deserve whatever they get... As you pointed out with an example from your 
own experience, if someone understands the application they should be able 
to fix a broken program. On the other hand, even if you are the best 
programmer in the world, and have no idea what the code should be doing, 
then results are less likely to be favourable.

The bottom line is that "easily maintainable" may well be a subjective 
illusion.

Know and understand the application, and code becomes easier to maintain, no 
matter what style it is written in.

Pete.
```

```
On Nov 2, 3:57 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[42 more quoted lines elided]…
> all the EXIT variants in COBOL we probably don't need them.

I consider, as outlined previously, that while the EXIT P/S/P
statements are a  huge improvement over GOTO they are still seriously
flawed because they depend on their position in the code.

> I don't know what you mean by a "section terminator"... for me that is
> simply the next section header. I usually stick a row of hyphens or
> something in there to make it more physically apparent.

It is, as you say, 'the next section header'. The problem arises if
the word 'section' is missed out, the compiler won't find this, it is
entirely a manual check.

>
> I think the problem here Richard, is that you are working on the assumption
> that a COBOL program can contain all kinds of mixed styles. You are right.
> Of course it can.

Not at all. I think mixed style are very bad idea. As I said a grep
should find no procedure sections, no goto, no THRU, no 'EXIT' except
EXIT PROGRAM/METHOD.
This cannot be a 'mixed style', it can only be one style.

>
> But my argument is predicated on a SPECIFIC style, laid down and enforced by
> system standards with peer review to ensure compliance.

Manually imposed by visual inspection. I want a program to tell me
what is wrong because they are more reliable.

>
> The style I use (and I re-iterate, I am not advocating it is the ONLY good
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> days...if I did, I might reconsider.)

I entirely agree that what you described was a vast improvement over
what went before. Your clarification above was not required, I already
accepted  that you had these reasonable and useful restrictions.

>
> Also I think I'd rather see a section with a series of GO TO MyExit
> statements in it than a series of EXIT SECTION statements. It's sort of like
> an expectation that the section must exit through the exit and EXIT SECTION
> means you are left "hanging" with regard to this expectation... :-)

I had already said that I dislike and do not use those EXITs, and gave
reasons.

> (I'm not totally serious about that; I feel quite sure that once I started
> using EXIT SECTION I'd become just as comfortable with it as I am with
> GO..TO exit. (Which I use extremely rarely, BTW...)

Don't use them, they are bug-traps.

> The bottom line is that no minds are likely to be changed because, as you
> are well aware, we all like what we are used to. Unless it was seriously
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

```
When IBM first introduced their '85 Standard compiler, SOME IBMers recommended 
the use of COPY statements for nested programs rather than separately compiled 
subroutines.  This always (almost always?) yielded significant performance 
improvements (depending on how many CALL were made).  The problem was a 
maintenance issue of knowing which programs needed recompiling when the COPY 
member changed.
```

```
On Wed, 12 Nov 2008 10:22:50 -0400, Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:

>On Tue, 11 Nov 2008 20:53:05 GMT, "William M. Klein"
><wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>That is the function of a good IDE and/or source management system.

Dependency trees in Windows IDEs and Unix makefiles are controlled by developers and/or
automatically generated.  Concurrent development (automated code merge) usually CANNOT be
turned off, for instance in CVS and SVN. 

The same functionality exists on mainframes (unbeknown to many developers), through
Endevor, Panvalet, Changeman, PVCS, etc., but dependency trees are disabled or out of date
and concurrent development is turned off. The reason is they are controlled by lazy
administrators rather than developers.

It's a management/political issue.
```

```
tlmfru wrote:
> 
> I didn't say I didn't use it.  I did say I don't like it.  Your statement
> "Cobol went beyond goto-less programming 20 years ago" is too generalized.
> The controversy started in the '70's, as I recall,

The canonical date is 1968, when Dijkstra's manuscript "A Case against
the GO TO Statement" was published in CACM as a letter under the title
(assigned by the editor) "Go-to statement considered harmful". This is
one of the most famous documents in computer science - it
inadvertently coined the "X considered harmful" snowclone, and caused
a flurry of controversy that still has not settled.

Edsger Dijkstra was (in)famously not fond of COBOL, and tended to hold
his opinions strongly, but the article is well worth reading. It's
only a few pages long.

It's available in numerous forms online, including:

	http://www.cs.utexas.edu/users/EWD/ewd02xx/EWD215.PDF

which I believe is a facsimile of the original typescript, typos and all.

> and has never been
> settled in a decisive sense - i.e,  a step-by-step logical argument which
> leads to an inescapable conclusion.

Under this rubric, has *any* non-tautological claim ever "been settled
in a decisive sense"?

I'm not sure exactly what the claim in question is, in this case, but
I suspect someone could make a strong pragmatist or epistemological
argument that it can't be proven in any manner that "leads to an
inescapable conclusion".

That said, Dijkstra's paper is pretty logical, though it is warranted
by speculations in cognitive science, since his argument is chiefly
about the comprehensibility of program code.

>  It HAS been proved that you can write
> perfectly well without using GOTO's - well, almost, because there was a
> lengthy discussion in this group some time ago about a verb to break out of
> loops - which was all about replacing a GOTO with something that did the
> same function but wasn't called a GOTO.

Regardless, any computable function can be written without GOTO. It
can be written without any sort of branching other than function calls
(and can in fact be written with only tail applications). It can be
written with nothing but primitive operations and the S and K
combinators, for that matter.

Of course, that doesn't mean that you'd *want* to write a given
program that way, or that you should. What you can do says little
about what would be a good idea.
```

```
Michael Mattias wrote:
> 
> Strange how "GO TO" has acquired such a reputation.
> 
> After all, it is fully twenty-five percent of the four (4) instructions to 

That's precisely the complaint: it's too low-level. People think
better when unnecessary detail is abstracted away, because there's a
small limit on the number of critical items we can pay attention to at
any given time.

We could all write software in assembly, too. But few people do,
because general-purpose software is just too complicated.

> which - it is said - all programming reduces:
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Jump

"Add" is redundant, since it can be implemented with cascading tests
and jumps. And you don't need to be able to do arbitrary sequences of
the other three. But you do need Load, so Jump is still 25%.

If the Church-Turing Thesis is correct, all computing reduces to the
basic operations of a Turing Machine:

Load-Store-Advance-Left
Load-Store-Advance-Right
Next-State

Load-Store* reads the contents of the current cell on the tape, writes
a new value there, and moves the pointer one cell to the left or the
right. Next-State chooses a state based on what was loaded, and
decides what will be stored on the next Load-Store instruction. These
can be decomposed into Stores, Loads, and Tests - but in particular
sequences.

All computable algorithms can also be implemented on a Post machine:

Read-Queue-Next-State
Write-Queue

Read-Queue-Next-State reads one character from the (FIFO) queue and
selects the next state based on what was read. Write-Queue writes the
specified character to the queue and goes to the next state (it can
only have one transition).

Again, these are in effect Load/Store/Test/Jump (or more precisely
Load/Store/Compare/Branch), but only in particular combinations.

Or an nPDA:

Push-n
Pop-n-Next-State
Read-Next-State

The nPDA has an input stream, which is read a character at a time by
the Read-Next-State instruction, and two or more stacks, each of which
can have one character pushed onto it or popped from it at a time.
(Push-1 pushes a character onto stack 1, and so on.)

Turing Machines, Post Machines, and nPDAs are equivalent. (And their
nondeterministic variants are no more powerful, in terms of the
computations they can perform, than their deterministic ones.)
```

```
written without use of transfers of control (i.e., failing to execute
statements in the order they're written).

follows that it is little different in spirit from a CALL or a PERFORM or
any of the other verbs.  The dirfference is that it's explicit.  Shakespeare
would probably not have said that a goto by any other name would still be a
goto.
```

```
On Nov 5, 6:48 pm, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
> Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote in message
>

> In the mathematical sense of proof - yes.  But in view of the fact that no
> word can be defined without using other words, which in turn can't be
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> of me to think that, I admit.

Do you have any examples of that ?

> Either "programs are better without GOTO's" or the absolutist "programs must
> be written without GOTO's".


> I agree with you that they can't be proved conclusively.  A true statement
> is that "writing programs without GOTO's will elminate the possibility of
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> goto.

Many anti-goto-less programmers seem to focus on the wrong aspects of
the use of go tos.

As I have already said, when examining a program to determine the flow
of control there is nothing wrong with goto. It is perfectly clear
what will happen at that point.

The problem is entirely that when looking at the code and there is a
label then, in a program that has gotos, it is not possible to know
the flow of control around that label without examining the whole
program for references to that label, and to the one above, and the
one above that.

Some standards attempt to make assurances about how flow is allowed to
occur by using signals. In the style under discussion, that used by
Peter, the SECTION keyword is an assurance that there will be no down-
down into this, the name-EXIT paragraph is an assurance that this is
the end and it should not drop-down into the next label, which should
be a SECTION.

However, there is no general automated mechanism that gives an
assurance that this is true except the programmer's claims to be
perfect. Or at least that 'peer review didn't find anything wrong'.

In other, modern, languages there is a _VAST_ difference between their
equivalent of a CALL and PERFORM and GO TO. Perhaps if you had learned
other languages you would appreciate this better.

Your claim that "it follows that it is little different in spirit from
a CALL or a PERFORM or any of the other verbs." is true of assembler
and COBOL and some early BASICs. It is not true of C or Pascal or
derivitives therefrom.

The reason that it is not true of other languages is that there are
_different_ label types and semantics for those labels. You cannot
goto a procedure or function, you cannot perform a branch label.

If i look at a C program and there is prcedure I know _exactly_ the
flow of control around that. I do not have to check to see how the
code gets to this point, there is only one way it can.

In order to make Cobol have some of the advantages of this is is
necessary to ensure that every label can only be accessed in one way.

It could be that if every transfer of control was an explicit GOTO
then this would satisfy the requirement. This would eliminate the use
of PERFORM and would make the program rather tedious, but it would
achieve the stated requirement.

Another way would be to only allow CALLs of nested procedures and
disallow any paragraph or section labels. In fact this is what could
follow in OO programs where METHODs being invoked is the only
mechanism used.

For COBOL programs not written nested or OO the restriction that each
paragraph only be used for one purpose (where the range is target of
goto, perform, thru, dropdown) then there is only one where this can
be verified by a general automated mechanism and doesn't rely on
visual inspection of the complete program and/or assurances of
perfection.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL ain't quite dead - yet !

_(thread depth: 16)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-06T13:29:16-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gf0djb1ige@news4.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<ace9d20f-4137-4554-aa45-dc79690f7884@w1g2000prk.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<SmnNk.790$Di1.171@newsfe13.iad> <ge50un$i5d$1@reader1.panix.com> <pan.2008.10.27.23.25.27.586401@localhost.localdomain> <S9uNk.28734$4u2.23175@newsfe01.iad> <7isgg4lh42b61712dijr6otf1otsscuj92@4ax.com> <aq%Nk.15914$o57.14136@newsfe02.iad> <6mrfc6Fh8lm7U2@mid.individual.net> <747hg4965hgegcuojhe10um7ic1f5lnccg@4ax.com> <6ms1tfFicn8eU1@mid.individual.net> <18e8bb27-c836-4a0e-b611-04c111a41ee8@r37g2000prr.googlegroups.com> <nPIOk.173$kd5.60@newsfe01.iad> <d0354600-0c9f-4979-ac64-84127e96412b@q26g2000prq.googlegroups.com> <gN0Pk.20824$nl3.7470@newsfe01.iad> <geptfa287r@news1.newsguy.com> <zLrQk.1153$ID5.77@newsfe01.iad> <ace9d20f-4137-4554-aa45-dc79690f7884@w1g2000prk.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
> 
> As I have already said, when examining a program to determine the flow
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> one above that.

I think it's worse than that. I believe the goto statement itself can
obscure the flow of control, in a number of plausible cases, even
though the operation of the goto is perfectly clear.

The problem, again, is that it interferes with abstraction.

Consider a loop that iterates over a list or array of numbers, adding
1 to each value.

The highest-level abstraction I can think of, off the top of my head,
is successive function application, as with the map and fold
operations typically provided by functional languages. You simply
write the equivalent of "do this to everything in the list". In LISP,
for example:

	[assume a function "add1" that adds 1 to a number]
	(mapcar 'add1 list)

will add 1 to every value in list. The loop is entirely abstracted away.

The second-highest is an automatic container iterator like the foreach
operator provided by some languages:

	foreach item in list
		do
		add 1 to item
		done

Here the loop is explicit, but the invariant is implicit in the
syntax. A maintainer looking at a foreach statement knows the intent:
there's some body that will be applied to every object in the collection.

Then we have well-formed loops with a single invariant and a looping
control structure. For example, in C:

	while (*item) *item++ += 1;

and at roughly the same level of abstraction, index-incrementing loops:

	[initialize "count" to number of items in list]
	perform varying idx from 1 by 1 until idx > count
		add 1 to list(idx)
	end-perform

Here the loop is explicit, and the invariant is explicit; but at least
the invariant is simple, and the mechanism by which it changes is
simple, and we're using a standard idiom that a maintainer can easily
recognize.

Goto statements, however, don't imply a loop; there are forward gotos,
and backward gotos that shouldn't cause a return to the goto
statement. And they say nothing about the invariant; instead, they
have to be wrapped in a branch. All of that decreases the level of
abstraction, and forces a human reading the source to look at more
context in order to discern control flow.

Here's an example:

		[initialize "count" to number of items in list]
		move 1 to idx
	loop-for-list.
		if idx not > count
			add 1 to item(idx)
			add 1 to idx
			go to loop-for-list
		end-if

The loop invariant is still pretty clear, but it's not obvious from
the syntax that it *is* the loop invariant. And it's modified several
lines later. And the go to and its target aren't clearly associated by
the syntax.

What was a one-word or one-line control structure has become several
lines, spread across the loop body.

And that's the best case. More often, a goto-based loop will have its
invariant test, invariant modification(s), and branching in various
places, with no obvious connections. So the maintainer has to keep
track of all of them to ferret out the control structure.

Gotos pose similar problems to software-processing programs, such as
optimizers. A foreach loop does one thing, which makes it easy to
optimize. Often the entire loop body is a single basic block. Gotos,
on the other hand, are general-purpose; they don't provide clues for
the optimizer. And they fragment the basic blocks, which reduces
opportunities for peephole and flow optimization.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL ain't quite dead - yet !

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-05T22:01:15-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<getndc0osd@news2.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<zLrQk.1153$ID5.77@newsfe01.iad>`
- **References:** `<SmnNk.790$Di1.171@newsfe13.iad> <ge50un$i5d$1@reader1.panix.com> <pan.2008.10.27.23.25.27.586401@localhost.localdomain> <S9uNk.28734$4u2.23175@newsfe01.iad> <7isgg4lh42b61712dijr6otf1otsscuj92@4ax.com> <aq%Nk.15914$o57.14136@newsfe02.iad> <6mrfc6Fh8lm7U2@mid.individual.net> <747hg4965hgegcuojhe10um7ic1f5lnccg@4ax.com> <6ms1tfFicn8eU1@mid.individual.net> <18e8bb27-c836-4a0e-b611-04c111a41ee8@r37g2000prr.googlegroups.com> <nPIOk.173$kd5.60@newsfe01.iad> <d0354600-0c9f-4979-ac64-84127e96412b@q26g2000prq.googlegroups.com> <gN0Pk.20824$nl3.7470@newsfe01.iad> <geptfa287r@news1.newsguy.com> <zLrQk.1153$ID5.77@newsfe01.iad>`

```
tlmfru wrote:
> Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message
> news:geptfa287r@news1.newsguy.com...
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> struck me that what he was talking about was checkpointing programs.

I'm not sure what "checkpointing" refers to here. Dijkstra's main
argument is that people are better at considering synchronic
arrangements (ones that don't change in time) than diachronic ones.
This is his cognitive-science claim. Given that, he says, it's better
if the human-readable representation of the program expresses the
process it implements in a static fashion.

Thus, it's better if a program abstraction says "this transforms X
into Y" than if it says "this does X, then Y".

And thus GO TO is a problem, because it's diachronic. And structured
programming is better, because it seeks to turn a program into a
series of functional atoms, each of which can be thought of as turning
one kind of data into another kind of data.

Now, we can certainly argue about whether he's right, but I think
that's definitely an argument against GO TO - that is, against flow
control that's not part of a synchronic abstraction (like "operate on
all the items of data in this collection").

> I remember reading at the time that studies had been done that purported to
> deminstrate that comments were just as effective in making code easier to
> understand, but that comments weren't as intellectually exciting as
> no-GOTO's.  Wish I'd kept it.

There may well have been. Personally, I find it hard to believe that
comments can compensate for a widespread lack of abstraction in
non-trivial programs, but I don't have any data on that.

>>> and has never been
>>> settled in a decisive sense - i.e,  a step-by-step logical argument
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> In the mathematical sense of proof - yes.

Any statement that can be proven mathematically is tautological; it's
just a transformation of the axioms that form the basis of the
mathematical system in question.

>  But in view of the fact that no
> word can be defined without using other words, which in turn can't be
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> of me to think that, I admit.

Sounds like a false dichotomy to me. I think there's some
epistemological wiggle room between pure logic and faith.

In other words, there might be arguments against GO TO that are not
provable, but are still reasonable.

That said, it's true that this is one of computing's religious wars,
and so there has on occasion been more heat than light.

> I agree with you that they can't be proved conclusively.  A true statement
> is that "writing programs without GOTO's will elminate the possibility of
> certain errors".  (Which is an example of a demonstrable proposition.)  All
> of which is to say, it behooves us not to be overly emphatic in our claims.

As indeed in most things...

>> Regardless, any computable function can be written without GOTO. It
>> can be written without any sort of branching other than function calls
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> statements in the order they're written).

You can make a stronger assertion: there's no conceptual machine
that's equivalent to a Turing Machine, which does not have a branching
mechanism. That's provable. In fact, even some regular languages can
only be accepted by machines that can branch.

But branching isn't the same as GOTO; GOTO is an arbitrary branch. You
can write any computable function (assuming the Church-Turing Thesis
holds) using only a rather restricted set of branches.

> If you accept a definition of GOTO's as a transfer of control

Alas, I do not.

That is, a GOTO is a transfer of control, but not all transfers of
control are GOTOs. In a strict computation-theory sense.

> then it
> follows that it is little different in spirit from a CALL or a PERFORM or
> any of the other verbs.

True, in a language like COBOL. CALL, PERFORM, etc are strictly more
powerful than a static GOTO, because they in effect implement a
dynamic GOTO when the called program or performed paragraph/section
return; but they're computationally equivalent under a
linearly-bounded space tradeoff. (You can replace each PERFORM with a
GOTO to a unique copy of the performed code that ends with a GOTO
returning to its "caller".)

In some languages, the difference among branching mechanisms is more
explicit, because they offer more powerful expressions. There's no
difference in computational power, but there's a tremendous difference
in expressiveness.

Continuation-passing style in functional languages, for example, or
pattern-matching in the ML family, don't look like GOTO, any more than
a satellite view of the US looks like what you see when you're
standing in the middle of Kansas.

I am not myself religious about GOTO. I have quite a lot of C code
that uses goto. It's used to enforce the single-point-of-return style:
every non-trival function has a "done" label near the end; there's
only one return statement, which is the last statement in the
function; and aborting the function (eg due to error) in the function
body is handled with a "goto done". That way, any necessary cleanup
(eg freeing temporary objects) happens once, between the "done" label
and the return statement.

Some people prefer to implement that style without goto, by having all
non-trivial functions called only by a wrapper, which calls the worker
and then does the cleanup. Same effect, for the price of an additional
call on the path. I think it's just as easy to use the gotos, and
because they're restricted to that one use, they don't interfere with
readability.

But in languages that have higher-level abstractions that accomplish
the same thing - such as try/catch/finally blocks - I use those rather
than goto. Abstract as much as is feasible.
```

```
By doing that, you've returned to everything global. You might as well write:

 02  customer-record.
```

```
In article <6lnng45rlarsqhpcfkj0sg2qeq8hfsr6pn@4ax.com>,
Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:
>On Thu, 30 Oct 2008 10:12:14 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"

>When I work under such a rule, I code the -EXIT paragraph, but never go

I'm not sure what you're calling 'the same', Mr Wagner.  Consider a 
program to process transactions across installations in a given region; a 
Classic Method - down to the CAPS LOCK, of course - might have it:

E07172-READ-N-PROCESS.

    PERFORM G76420-READ-TRANS-FILE THRU G76420-RTF-EX.
    IF NO-MORE-TRANSACTIONS
       GO TO E07172-RNP-EX
    END-IF

    IF NOT VALID-REGION-NO
        MOVE '**** INVALID REGION NO IN E07172 FILE'    TO ERRMSG-1
        MOVE  E07172-REC-CTR TO ERR-REC-CTR
        MOVE 'REGION NO IN FILE BUT NOT VERIFY-TBL'     TO ERRMSG-3
        MOVE '**** CONTACT PROGRAMMING & ACCOUNTING'    TO ERRMSG-3
        MOVE '**** TERMINATING RUN - ENJOY THE EVENING' TO ERRMSG-4
        MOVE 878 TO RETURN-CODE
        GO TO 9999-ABEND-RTN
    END-IF.

    IF SOUTHERN-REGION
        PERFORM E1000-DIXIE-STUFF THRU E1000-DS-EX
        GO TO E07172-RNP-EX
    END-IF.

    IF MIDWEST-REGION
        PERFORM E2000-CORN-BELT  THRU E20000-EX
        IF GULF-COAST
              PERFORM E3000-KATRINA-DEVALUATION
                 THRU E3000-KD-EX
        END-IF
        GO TO E07172-RNP-EX
    END-IF.

... et and cetera.  Now, see... it contains scope-delimiters for those who 
just *have* to have the latest and greatest in twenty-years old 
constructs, it has periods so you can stuff in new... stuff in the middle 
of things and know where the effects will end AND those handy GO TOs save 
you time and money on your condition checks... those IFs, minimally, cost 
you a CLI and those pile up after a while.

Now sure, someone can say 'Pfooie... that's how folks coded thirty years 
ago!'... to which the response might be 'yep... might there be something 
in the water now-a-days which would make it not work?'

>
>>but it served to tie up everything 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>You never need it, if the program is written well.

You never need a fire extinguisher, if buildings are made well.

>
>>It costs you nothing...
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>header, and the time to enforce a pointless rule.

No rule which results in a paycheck may be said to be 'pointless', Mr 
Wagner... and if a couple of extra lines of code in order to be in line 
with shop standards is too much for a person then it might be that such a 
person is Too Delicate for the situation and would do better finding work 
elsewhere.

DD
```

```
On Sat, 1 Nov 2008 08:01:32 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <6lnng45rlarsqhpcfkj0sg2qeq8hfsr6pn@4ax.com>,
>Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
>in the water now-a-days which would make it not work?'

I would have written it this way:

    PERFORM G76420-READ-TRANSACTION THRU G76420-EXIT
    PERFORM UNTIL END-OF-TRANSACTIONS
        PERFORM E07172-ONE-TRANSACTION THRU E07172-EXIT
        PERFORM G76420-READ-TRANSACTION THRU G76420-EXIT
    END-PERFORM
    IF  RETURN-CODE NOT EQUAL TO ZERO
         GO TO 9999-ABORT
    END-IF


. E07172-ONE-TRANSACTION.
      EVALUATE REGION
          WHEN SOUTHERN-REGION
              PERFORM E1000-DIXIE-STUFF THRU E1000-EXIT
         WHEN MIDWEST-REGION
              PERFORM E2000-CORN-BELT  THRU E20000-EXIT
              IF GULF-COAST
                  PERFORM E3000-KATRINA-DEVALUATION THRU E3000-EXIT
             END-IF
        WHEN NOT VALID-REGION
             MOVE '**** INVALID REGION NO IN E07172 FILE'    TO ERRMSG-1
             MOVE  E07172-REC-CTR TO ERR-REC-CTR
             MOVE REGION TO ERRMSG-3
             PERFORM 1234-PRINT-TO-LOG THRU 1234-EXIT
             MOVE 878 TO RETURN-CODE
     END-EVALUATE
. E07172-EXIT.          
    EXIT

Style guide:

.. State the normal, most common case first, exceptions at the end
.. A paragraph does one thing, in this case process one transaction.
   Reading the next is flow control. It has nothing to do with processing the current.
.. Log all errors, don't quit on the first.

>>>but it served to tie up everything 
>>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>You never need a fire extinguisher, if buildings are made well.

Allowing a  called program to abort the job is like a empowering file clerk to declare the
company bankrupt.

>>>It costs you nothing...
>>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>elsewhere.

Policy decides the fate of systems and institutions; politics decides the fate of
individual people. My domain is policy; managers deal with politics. 

From what I've seen, their track record is poor. When they fire someone, they replace him
with one just like the first. The error is repeated a few months later. Retribution makes
them feel good, but doesn't fix the system.

My job is to FIX THE SYSTEM, not to make middle managers feel good.
```

```
Agreed.  Be prepared for the most common first (the bell-curve's belly) 
and you'll most often be most efficient.  There are, usually, more 
transactoions than stores, more stores than areas, more areas than 
regions, more regions than divisions.

>processing the current.

I disagree.  Break-processing is determined by the next record; whether 
the current record causes data to be ADDED to CURR-TOTAL or PREV-TOTAL 
depends on what went on before.

>.. Log all errors, don't quit on the first.

I disagree.  If the first error shows that the file's header record does 
not match the current processing date then blowing up can be a Very Good 
Thing... unless people *like* last week's data processed as this weeks.  
Different kinds of errors are determined (by Policy, of course) as having 
different weight... once the weight has been reached then it is time to GO 
TO 9999-ABEND.

>clerk to declare the

data change, unforseen conditions work their was into crevices and a 
program blows.  If your hypothetical clerk has bankruptcy-declaring power 
then she will have equal business-improving priveleges... works both ways, 
I'd say.

[snip]

>My job is to FIX THE SYSTEM, not to make middle managers feel good. 

My job is to bring the decades of my experience to bear ona problem and 
let those who are responsible for the allocation, co-ordionation and 
motivation of personnel and resources to decide what need to be done 
next... remember, a Well-Functioning system is one which causes the person 
who signs the checks for it to smile.
```

```
"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:6lnng45rlarsqhpcfkj0sg2qeq8hfsr6pn@4ax.com...
> On Thu, 30 Oct 2008 10:12:14 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" 
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

specifically. It would always t=fall into it.

>

>
> What does that mean?

It means there is ONE, SPECIFIC, DOCUMENTED exit from a section, and control 
flow will always leave by that exit and ONLY by that exit.
>
>>and was there should you ever need it.
>
> You never need it, if the program is written well.

Probably. Nevertheless,. as Doc pointed out, you don't need a fire 
extinguisher if a building is well built. I code it because it makes me feel 
better... I don't need any other reason... :-)
>
>>It costs you nothing...
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> header, and the time to enforce a pointless rule.

In my case it is copied from a template so that isn't true... It is a 
single, or at most, two, mouse clicks.

>
>> This is very
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> How? The section name contains no more information than a paragraph name.

No, but the section references an entire fuction. You can't always achieve 
that with a single paragraph. Usnng SECTIONs (provided they are used wisely) 
logically delineates functionality. Sure, paragrphs can do that to, but 
sometimes you want to to delineate several paragraphs under the same 
"umbrella". SECTIONing lets you do this.

>
> Comments are the place to document functionality.

Yes, I agree. Unfortunately they are not referenceable...
>
>>A series of paragraphs is just a program. A series of SECTIONS delineates
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> its inputs and outputs are.

I'm not arguing nested programs here. I agree they are very useful.
>
>>Some of us who were fond of PERFORM... THROUGH grumbled a bit and pointed
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> The same arguments apply to nested functions.

I know that, Robert. I've been using nested programs for many years. However 
the topic under discussion is currently SECTIONs.

Fujitsu PowerCOBOL implements OO COBOL by means of nested programs and 
external data blocks.
>
>>For "internal" (private) methods
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Almost. That is certainly the case in PowerCOBOL. However, it is not exactly 
the same when you use COM throughFujitsu's Base COM class. This Class 
provides support for  GETters and SETters that are part of a COM interface 
ONLY. It is not the same as EXTERNAL at all.

I understand your objections but you missed some fundamental points here:

1. We are discussing SECTIONed code in COBOL, not Nested Programs in COBOL.
2. Private Methods and Properties in Fujitsu's OO COBOL are implemented 
differently between COM and PowerCOBOL.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL ain't quite dead - yet !

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-02T10:53:03-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9e878d75-e9e4-4cae-8e89-37c76a484991@40g2000prx.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<SmnNk.790$Di1.171@newsfe13.iad> <ge50un$i5d$1@reader1.panix.com> <pan.2008.10.27.23.25.27.586401@localhost.localdomain> <S9uNk.28734$4u2.23175@newsfe01.iad> <7isgg4lh42b61712dijr6otf1otsscuj92@4ax.com> <aq%Nk.15914$o57.14136@newsfe02.iad> <6mrfc6Fh8lm7U2@mid.individual.net> <747hg4965hgegcuojhe10um7ic1f5lnccg@4ax.com> <6ms1tfFicn8eU1@mid.individual.net> <6lnng45rlarsqhpcfkj0sg2qeq8hfsr6pn@4ax.com> <6n5gqqFjuo9gU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Nov 3, 12:21 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Robert" <n...@e.mail> wrote in message
>
…[96 more quoted lines elided]…
> "umbrella". SECTIONing lets you do this.

So you have several paragraph labels within a section. You say you
only perform a section so what _function_ do these labels perform ?
target of goto ?

If they were comments, by just adding an '*' in column 7 then the
wording would still be there but there would be no confusion about
whether they were, or could be, referenced.

One of the issues that I have with Cobol that puts it in the class of
program languages that is 'archaic' is that the labels are used like
those in assembler languages of the 50s.

In Cobol a label may be the target of a goto, a perform, a drop
through. There is nothing at the point of the label that indicates
which of these may occur.

I C and most other languages (and including nested program names) a
particular type of label can only be used for one thing. A function or
procedure can only be invoked, it cannot be dropped into or subject to
a goto. There are labels within switch (evaluate) and also labels that
may be gotoed, but they can't be used otherwise.

By restricting the ways that labels can be used, in effect eliminating
all alternatives, and by eliminating all unnecessary (unreferenced)
labels, then the necessity of checking each label is reduced.

While you 'know' that you never perform a paragraph label or use a
goto and thus never need to check, the next programmer doesn't know
that, or it may have gone through other hands before he has it. Thus
every label needs to be checked in order to determine the logic flow
through the code. If the 'paragraph label' is a comment then there is
nothing to check, the compiler will complain if it is used as a
target.

Several years ago I proposed to J4 that labels should have keywords
that indicated they were restricted in usage. Then the compiler could
indicate where this was not observed.

For example the keywords would be PERFORMED, FROM, ABOVE and indicated
what was allowed. ABOVE was a drop through.

      my-section SECTION PERFORMED.
      my-section-first ABOVE.

          ...
      my-section-EXIT ABOVE.
         EXIT.
      next-section PERFORMED.
      next-first NONE.

          ....

Would give an error, as would a GO TO.


As support for this I developed a measure of program complexity that
was the sum of all labels with the number of different ways that the
label was referenced.

If a label was only dropped through then that was 1. If it was dropped
through and there was a goto it then that counted 2. If it also was
the target of a thru then it counted 3.

      my-section SECTION.
      my-section-first.

          .... go to my-section-EXIT.

          ...
      my-section-EXIT.
         EXIT.

This has complexity factor of 4. Adding an explicit GO TO my-section-
EXIT immediately before that paragraph reduces the complexity factor
to 3.

This factor roughly equates to the amount of 'obvious checking'
required when a programmer is given code they have not seen before.

You may **KNOW** that the checks are not required. Unfortunately the
only way that you can communicate that _in_the_code_ is by not having
labels that could possibly be used.


> > Comments are the place to document functionality.
>
> Yes, I agree. Unfortunately they are not referenceable...

So, you do use GOTOs, or perform paragraphs within a section ?

If you write Cobol as you would assembler then it remains an archaic
language and _should_ be eliminated.

It seems that you wrote Cobol almost exclusively and then 'discovered'
C# and were much more productive. My theory is that it wasn't that C,
or C# or Java is so much better, it was that the way Cobol programs
were traditionally (archaicly) written was so much worse.

In the 60s the programmers were used to assembler, so they had an
excuse. In the 70s there were hardware limits so there was an excuse,
in the 80s there were no scope terminators so there was an excuse.

> >>A series of paragraphs is just a program. A series of SECTIONS delineates
> >>specific functions more visibly, in my opinion.)
…[73 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:9e878d75-e9e4-4cae-8e89-37c76a484991@40g2000prx.googlegroups.com...
> On Nov 3, 12:21 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> So you have several paragraph labels within a section.

Sometimes...


> You say you
> only perform a section so what _function_ do these labels perform ?
> target of goto ?

None, other than to delineate a sub function.

Something along the lines...
*---------------------------------------------
calc-payment  section.
get-hours.
      ....db access
get-rate.
      .... different db access
do-calc.
      compute allowances
      compute deductions
      compute any special taxes
      calculate pay
cp-exit.
      exit.
*-----------------------------------------

I agree that comments could serve equally well. But suppose those paragraphs 
were actually much larger than shown here?

I want to find the bit that deals with rates.

It stands out more if it is a paragraph.

In practise, My sections usually look like...

*---------------------------------------------
calc-payment  section.
cp000.
      ....db access for hours
      .... different db access for rate
      ...compute allowances
      ...compute deductions
      ...compute any special taxes
      ...calculate pay
cp999.
      exit.
*-----------------------------------------

... except there would be comments.

This is fine if the section is small (which most of mine are...)

If the section extended to more than 100 or so lines of code, I would 
probably add paragraphs, as shown above, or consider breaking some of the 
larger functions to their own sections.


>
> If they were comments, by just adding an '*' in column 7 then the
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> those in assembler languages of the 50s.

I wrote assembler for mainframes in the 60s and for PCs in the early 80s... 
maybe I picked up some habits without actually realising it.

>
> In Cobol a label may be the target of a goto, a perform, a drop
> through. There is nothing at the point of the label that indicates
> which of these may occur.

No, you made this point a long time ago and suggested a labelling convention 
that could determine this, so programs can be autochecked. (You see, I do 
take note of what you write... :-)) I agree it is a good idea. I agreed the 
first time you suggested it. However, it means a change of style and more 
effort when coding to remember which particular usuage a label is going to 
have.
>
> I C and most other languages (and including nested program names) a
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> indicate where this was not observed.

Yes I remember... Did you ever get a response?

>
> For example the keywords would be PERFORMED, FROM, ABOVE and indicated
…[42 more quoted lines elided]…
> labels that could possibly be used.

I don't deny it. The question is whether checks "_in_the_code_" are actually 
required, if I KNOW that checks are not required. :-)

If I worked on a site where programs were running out of control because of 
incorrect PERFORMs or GO...TO, I'd be paying much more attention and looking 
to do something about it. I'm not. And this has not been a problem on most 
of the sites where I have worked. Such events are picked up in testing or 
peer review. If site standards require constrained use of PERFORM and no use 
of GO TO, then the likelihood is minimised.

I wouldn't mind betting that, for example, on IBM mainframe sites where 
COBOL is used, there are many more production failures due to 0C7 (data) 
exceptions than to 0C4 (addressing) or endless looping.

>
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> So, you do use GOTOs, or perform paragraphs within a section ?

No. I already said that I don't. (I can think of one possible exception 
which doesn't happen very often, but, in general, no) The remark above means 
that you cannot reference comments visually once they are "Off the page".

Here is the exception case I mentioned... It is to do with filtering and 
flushing unwanted records in a sequential stream.

*---------------------------------------------
seq-process  section.
init.
      ....set up.
get-input.
      get a record... exit with a flag set if EOF...
      is it a record we want? yes, continue process...
      no...GO TO get-input
other-processes.
      ...
sp-exit.
      exit.
*-----------------------------------------

These days I would probably PERFORM UNTIL  (got what I'm looking for OR EOF) 
but it still represents a case where, technically, control might be 
transferred to a label within the section. (I think the last time I did this 
was about 10 yeas ago...)

>
> If you write Cobol as you would assembler then it remains an archaic
> language and _should_ be eliminated.
>
I don't. My Assembler is much worse... :-)

> It seems that you wrote Cobol almost exclusively and then 'discovered'
> C# and were much more productive. My theory is that it wasn't that C,
> or C# or Java is so much better, it was that the way Cobol programs
> were traditionally (archaicly) written was so much worse.

Certainly I did not use many high level languages other than COBOL  (some 
PL/1, some Fortran, some BASIC and ICL JEAN (which was a close relative of 
BASIC).  I don't believe this had anything to do with increased productivity 
in C# and Java; I believe that was a function of the language being less 
verbose and the toolset being infinitely better. I can't really prove it and 
I don't really care... I'm just happy that I can do more now in a shorter 
time than I could in COBOL.

The point you seem unable to admit is that if code is NOT problematic, 
no-one is going to do anything about it.

Whether you like the way I code or not, it simply isn't problematic. I don't 
have problems with it, and neither has anyone else in the decades where I 
wrote it for a living.

Your objections are valid, but they are theoretical. In practice, most shops 
address this with standards, peer review, and testing. BECAUSE they do, they 
don't encounter the problems you posit. Because they don't have the problems 
they are (like me) unlikely to change the way they do things.

> In the 60s the programmers were used to assembler, so they had an
…[95 more quoted lines elided]…
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Any Freelance Cobol Programmers working out of Malaysia?

```
My experience in New Zealand is that for someone that is any good it is more 
like $75-$100 /hour.
For the high end rate you are generally looking for a degree of input in the 
system analysis area and hopefully a rare programmer who can interpret user 
feedback at a deeper level than what they are actually being told.
Fixed quotes has always been the way I have worked in the past 15+ years. 
Your rate is then of no concern for me, the quality of your work and your 
ability to meet a fixed timeframe is the only thing of consequence. This 
brings cost certainty to projects which is what is fundementally what 
clients want. My job is always to protect the programmer from having to deal 
with a moving target. Feedback from programmers I have had is that I do that 
well.
This coming project I will try to break down into small enough portions to 
lessen the risk of programmers going over their time estimates.
I run Microfocus products on a windows 2008 terminal server as well as a 
Centos virtual machine with NX client access with VNC server running for 
remote demonstrations. I have Redmine for project planning, issue tracking 
and user submissions as well as holding my SVN repositories. This helps with 
good written communication. I am currently setting up an asterisk VoIP 
server with a variety of SIP accounts to hopefully improve the quality of 
verbal communication with programmers around the world. I use Xoom and 
Paypal for payment although for Malaysian and I guess NZ programmers, direct 
credit from a local bank account will be possible. Anyone is welcome to add 
their name to my pool of resources if they feel they can provide value for 
money.
```

<a id="t-416a2b2822"></a>
## Thread: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers — 131 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2008-11-03T10:54:00-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d2GPk.7270$cx5.2876@newsfe01.iad>`

```
I can't resist revisiting this a bit.

Programmers who don't like or don't use GOTO's are fond of pointing out the
pitfalls of using the labels that are required: ALTERING a label that is
PERFORMED; falling through a PERFORMed label, etc. etc.; the general thesis
being that since there are several verbs that involve labels programmers who
use GOTO's may well use several verbs on a given label.  Just out of
curiosity, and in a wholly unscientific manner, since no accurate statistics
are possible unless every active Cobol programmer responds, I'd like to get
some ideas on the prevalence of these bad habits.

Note that I am ONLY interested in whether or how often these things are
done.  I have NO interest in commenting on them.

Therefore, I have several questions for those who use GOTO's::

1a - do you ever use ALTERs?
1b - if so, do you ever ALTER a PERFORMed label?
1c - do you ever fall through a label that can be ALTERed?
1d - how often do you come across ALTERs in programs you have to maintain?

2a - do you ever GOTO and PERFORM the same label?
2b - do you ever fall through a PERFORMed label?

3a - do you use both SECTIONS and paragraph labels in the same program?
(Exception: SORT INPUT/OUTPUT procedures don't need to be mentioned).
3b - do you always use a terminating label (i.e., 999-X. EXIT.) to indicate
the end of a PERFORMed paragraph, or a SECTION?  Or sometimes, or never?
3c - do you always, or sometimes, or never, use PERFORM ...THRU i.e., as
opposed to just PERFORM?

4a - do you ever use GOTO DEPENDING ON?
4b - if so, do you ever ALTER any of the target labels?
4c - are any of the target labels also PERFORMed?
4d - are any of the target labels also the destination of an unconditional
GOTO?

My own answers are:

1a - no; 1b,c - n/a; 1d - not for a long time

2a, b - never

3a - sort i/o procedures only.
3b, c- always.

4a- occasionally
4b, c - never
4d - in my youth I did.  Not for a long time, though.

I may come up with other questions.

Again, the object of the exewrcise is just to determine what practices
exist, and therefore (hopefully) to keep discussion of the topic to what IS
rather than what MIGHT BE.

Thanks
PL
```

#### ↳ Re: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers
