(my first post in this group, as compact as possible)

<<snip>>

> Therefore, I have several questions for those who use GOTO's::

 [N] 1a - do you ever use ALTERs?
 [N] 1b - if so, do you ever ALTER a PERFORMed label?
 [N] 1c - do you ever fall through a label that can be ALTERed?
 [N] 1d - how often do you come across ALTERs in programs you have to
       maintain?

 [N] 2a - do you ever GOTO and PERFORM the same label?
 [N] 2b - do you ever fall through a PERFORMed label?

 [Y] 3a - do you use both SECTIONS and paragraph labels in the same
       program? (Exception: SORT INPUT/OUTPUT procedures don't need to
       be mentioned).
 [Y] 3b - do you always use a terminating label (i.e., 999-X.
       EXIT.) to indicate the end of a PERFORMed paragraph, or a
       SECTION? Or sometimes, or never?
     Note : always, its name is EOS (end of section) and it is only used
            (if it's used) in a GO TO in the same section.
 [N] 3c - do you always, or sometimes, or never, use PERFORM ...THRU
       i.e., as opposed to just PERFORM?
     Note : never.

 [Y] 4a - do you ever use GO TO DEPENDING ON?
     Note : exceptionally
 [N] 4b - if so, do you ever ALTER any of the target labels?
 [N] 4c - are any of the target labels also PERFORMed?
 [N] 4d - are any of the target labels also the destination of an
       unconditional GO TO?

#### ↳ Re: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-03T10:52:10-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<42717fc6-8b98-481f-8886-6b200d24b95c@t18g2000prt.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<d2GPk.7270$cx5.2876@newsfe01.iad>`

```
On Nov 4, 5:54 am, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
> I can't resist revisiting this a bit.
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> some ideas on the prevalence of these bad habits.

I think that you have asked most of the right questions. But what is
required is not answered by asking programmers but by 'asking' the
compiler or some other program.

My concerns are not so much with a program that is on your desk but
only if it is put on my desk. At that point I want these answers in
respect of the code that I will have to work with.

In a site that has standards and peer review and authoritarian
management there is a good chance that the code will follow the
standard, unless it is rather old of course. But much code is not
recent or has not been written and rewritten and modified under tight
control.

If a set of programs arrives on my desk to be dealt with then it is
not relevant that your survey finds 92% of programmers do not use
ALTER, or don't often use GO TO. It is only relevant if I can get
those answers to the code that I am looking at.

If I can grep the source and find no occurrence of 'ALTER' then I need
to look no further. If I could also find no 'GO' then that is a few
days worth of checking that I do not need to do.

I may well have a note with the code that states that all the code has
been written perfectly and there are no incorrect labels or mistaken
go tos, but, cynic that I am, I want the compiler or similar to tell
me that, even peer review can miss problems.

(actually if it had been written perfectly they wouldn't give it to
me.)


> Note that I am ONLY interested in whether or how often these things are
> done.  I have NO interest in commenting on them.
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> rather than what MIGHT BE.

Then you miss the point completely.

When I have a new pile of source code it is irrelevant what IS on your
desk, or Peter's, the issue is entirely what MIGHT BE within the code
in that pile.

I am saying that Cobol is treated as an archaic language because with
typical styles as described by Peter there is no mechanism that can
answer your questions automatically by examining the code. Certainly,
the style introduces 'signals' such as an end-of-section-exit-
paragraph is a signal that the next label _MUST_ have a SECTION
keyword. But these are all visual and must be laboriously checked
manually to ensure compliance.

Perhaps some checks could be automated but that would probably require
that naming conventions had been used - and then you need some way of
assuring that those had been.

As it happens I have been given a couple of hundred programs where I
have discovered some errors. The program have been developed over a
quarter century and many were originally '74.  The programmer did not
fill out your questionaire in respect of each program so I will have
to determine those answers myself, laboriously.

So the real question is: if I received one of your programs how would
I determine the answers to your questions without having to read every
line and follow every path ?

If I knew that it was you that had written it, and no one else since,
and the peer review procedures and standards of your site I could take
it on trust and would only need to check those bits that needed
changing and keep to your ways.

But if it arrives on my desk and I don't know the author I can only
judge from the code itself, and with Cobol (for reasons explained)
that requires lots of hard work.
```

>1a - do you ever use ALTERs?
Of course not.

Not in >30 years.

>2a - do you ever GOTO and PERFORM the same label?
No. Do not use go to.
>2b - do you ever fall through a PERFORMed label?
No. Never fall through. Those who use perform thru exit paragraph must answer yes. 

>3a - do you use both SECTIONS and paragraph labels in the same program?

No. Do not use sections. Prefer nested programs over paragraphs.

Never.

No. That's what EVALUATE is for.

No.
>4c - are any of the target labels also PERFORMed?
No.

No.

#### ↳ Re: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2008-11-04T05:02:27+00:00

```
tlmfru wrote:
> (snip) 
> 
> Therefore, I have several questions for those who use GOTO's::
> 
> 1a - do you ever use ALTERs?

No.

> 1b - if so, do you ever ALTER a PERFORMed label?
> 1c - do you ever fall through a label that can be ALTERed?
> 1d - how often do you come across ALTERs in programs you have to maintain?

We retired out last such program in 1992.

> 
> 2a - do you ever GOTO and PERFORM the same label?

No

> 2b - do you ever fall through a PERFORMed label?

No

> 
> 3a - do you use both SECTIONS and paragraph labels in the same program?
> (Exception: SORT INPUT/OUTPUT procedures don't need to be mentioned).

No.  I only use paragraphs, even for SORT INPUT/OUTPUT procedures.

> 3b - do you always use a terminating label (i.e., 999-X. EXIT.) to indicate
> the end of a PERFORMed paragraph, or a SECTION?  Or sometimes, or never?

In my own coding I never use any terminating label.  I maintain a few 
older programs where some (not all) paragraphs have an EXIT label.

> 3c - do you always, or sometimes, or never, use PERFORM ...THRU i.e., as
> opposed to just PERFORM?

I support a few programs that have one or two instances of PERFORM THRU 
EXIT.  In other words, most paragraphs are only performed, and a few are 
performed thru an exit.  Generally this is a single paragraph and GO TO 
EXIT is used to bypass any other logic in that paragraph.  In my own 
coding there are no EXIT paragraphs, no section headers, no THRU EXIT, 
and no GO TO statements.
> 
> 4a - do you ever use GOTO DEPENDING ON?

No, and I haven't seen it used in a production program in more than 20 
years.

> 4b - if so, do you ever ALTER any of the target labels?
> 4c - are any of the target labels also PERFORMed?
> 4d - are any of the target labels also the destination of an unconditional
> GOTO?
> 

I would have to agree with Richard Plinston that it simplifies things if 
there are no GO TO statements or EXIT paragraphs or sections.  I can 
still make mistakes even without using them.
```

```
On Nov 6, 6:23 am, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
> In article <5lk3h4d55e67bsvkftdtfqps3dmvip3...@4ax.com>,
>         Robert <n...@e.mail> writes:

> Both Paragraphs are explicitly mentioned.  No GOTO.  No Fall-thru.

You misunderstand what 'fall into', or fall thru, means.

The actual answer, based on your example, is b. always fall into.

That is, after the ADD 1 the next thing that happens is that the
program 'falls into' the Count-Exit paragraph.

In fact the PERFORM a THRU z specifies exactly that the program should
start at a and (fall) thru until the end of z.

Your answer appeared to be as if your PERFORM was

    PERFORM COUNT-PARA
    PERFORM COUNT-EXIT

which is a completely different beast.
```

  The Standard (and all compilers that I know of) allow for:

Perform Para2 thru Para1
   ...
Para2.
   Display "second".
Para1.
   Display "first".

  Perform Para2 thru Para1

  Perform Para1 thru Para2

```
On Nov 7, 3:50 pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.netcom.com>
wrote:
> Richard,
>   The Standard (and all compilers that I know of) allow for:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>   Perform Para2 thru Para1

Please re-read your message and correct it.

I do not know why you got this backwards.

Please reread my message and understand it.


> (Why is one a "fall-thru" and the other isn't)

Robert's questions referred to fall-INTO.  Given:

   PERFORM para-a thru para-z

   para-a.
       ...
   para-b.
       ...
   ...
       ...
   para-z.
       ...

and no gotos then each para- label between the start of para-a and the
end of para-z will be fallen into and fallen-thru until the end of
para-z cause a return to the statement after the perform.

Note: "fall" is always in a downward direction.

> If you do consider
>   Perform Para1 thru Para2
> to be fall-thru, I think you find that many (possibly most) COBOL programmers
> disagree with your use of the term.

But then most cobol programmers wouldn't misunderstand so badly what I
actually wrote.

>>>> Falling thru is when you
>>>> change paragraphs with no command telling it to do that.

Even with perform para-a thru para-a-exit

   para-a
      (no goto)
   para-a-exit
       exit.

there is _NO_ command to tell the code to change to para-a-exit, it
simply falls off the end of para-a and falls (downwards) into para-a-
exit, or into any intermediate, and falls-thru until it reaches the
end of para-a-exit.

The only case where this example code wouldn't fall-tru is if there
was an explicit go to para-a-exit at the end of para-a.


> --
> Bill Klein
…[64 more quoted lines elided]…
> program 'falls into' the Count-Exit paragraph.

As clarification for Bill: this explanation is of the actual code
sample above where there are no intermediate paragraphs. If there were
paragraphs between COUNT-PARA and COUNT-EXIT then it would fall-thru
those too.


> In fact the PERFORM a THRU z specifies exactly that the program should
> start at a and (fall) thru until the end of z.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> which is a completely different beast.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-10T02:50:53+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<hkNRk.145893$cU2.30642@fe04.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<d2GPk.7270$cx5.2876@newsfe01.iad> <vvv0h45okn0ta3afojtnh66l6cd9crh1un@4ax.com> <fj12h4pe2rhi2veb7ibgop6i4l319vdd8a@4ax.com> <6ndi1eFkoho1U1@mid.individual.net> <5mg3h4h2jp2irqhf449n9531c0ls00pf6s@4ax.com> <5lk3h4d55e67bsvkftdtfqps3dmvip3tgj@4ax.com> <6ne34vFl6rj3U1@mid.individual.net> <7d19d384-fefa-44dc-ab59-b0319919f08b@s9g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <e2OQk.69329$Fs5.13368@fe10.news.easynews.com> <3890aac7-c251-4ac8-a2b9-4980735828c7@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard,
  Not worth responding in detail.  You use of "fall-thru" is NOT the same as 
mine (or that of anyone that I can remember ever working with.)

In the case of
  Perform  Para1 thru Para3
with a structure of

 Para1.
    ....
Para2.
   ...
Para3.

Then there IS a "fall-thru" (or fall into if you prefer) from Para1 to Para2. 
There is NOT a fall thru from Para2 to Para3 as Para3 is part of the explicit 
PERFORM statement.

I understand your (repeated) view that this isn't true, but that does NOT mean 
that this is the common usage of "fall thru" (or fall into) as used by those 
COBOL programmers that I have worked with.

I have certainly seen several others posting in this group who agree with my 
understanding of this term. I would be curious if there are others 
reading/participating in CLC who would think that in
  Perform PARAx thru PARAy
that the "entrance" into PARAy is *ever* a "fall thru". (or fall into or any 
other type of "fall").

If I am in error in reading YOUR note (not Robert's) and you agree that the 
entry into PARAy is NOT a "fall into/thru", then I apologize for my 
misunderstanding.
```

```
On Nov 10, 3:50 pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.netcom.com>
wrote:
> Richard,
>   Not worth responding in detail.  You use of "fall-thru" is NOT the same as
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> PERFORM statement.

The mechanism of progressing from the end para1 to the start of para2
and from the end of para2 to the start of para3 is _identical_. Why
would you wave your hands and say 'no,no, this is not drop-xx' ?.

The only relevant part of Para3, because it is in the THRU clause of
the  PERFORM, is the bit that is _after_ all the statements because
that is where the return is from.

As Bill (the other one) clearly stated: "Falling thru is when you
change paragraphs with no command telling it to do that."

Where is the 'command' at the end of Para2 that you think changes the
paragraph to Para3 ?  (Clue: It isn't the PERFORM).



> I understand your (repeated) view that this isn't true, but that does NOT mean
> that this is the common usage of "fall thru" (or fall into) as used by those
> COBOL programmers that I have worked with.

Then they are all wrong. There is no magic in the code at the end of
Para2 (in the example) that makes the transfer travel via some 4th
dimension. The process simply drops off the end of Para2 exactly as it
drops off Para1 and drops-into Para3. It actually drops-through all
the code in Para3 until it finds the return after the end of the code


> I have certainly seen several others posting in this group who agree with my
> understanding of this term. I would be curious if there are others
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> other type of "fall").

Regardless of how many vote on its meaning, it _IS_ a fall from the
end of Para1, and from Para2, into the next paragraph.


> If I am in error in reading YOUR note (not Robert's) and you agree that the
> entry into PARAy is NOT a "fall into/thru", then I apologize for my
> misunderstanding.

At the end of Para1 it falls into the next paragraph.

It will continue to do this until it reaches a point after the _END_
of the paragraph specified in the THRU.

The PERFORM statement is explicit is specifying this as a THRU. ie it
is:

   PERFORM (starting at) Para1 (and falling) THRU (until the end of)
Para3



> --
> Bill Klein
…[154 more quoted lines elided]…
> >> which is a completely different beast.
```

```
On Mon, 10 Nov 2008 10:22:42 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>The range invoked by the THRU causes the code in Para3 to be executed, Mr 
>Plinston... are you saying that range-execution is the same as falling 
>through?

I would.   The THRU doesn't say how the code gets from the first
paragraph to the end of the PERFORM.

>If that is the case then how is the code which follows the PERFORM 
>executed?

When the code reaches the end of the PERFORM THRU, the PERFORM takes
control again.   But until it reaches the end, it can do all sorts of
things, including falling through paragraphs.

I wonder what would happen if someone tried to eliminate GO TO by
replacing the GO TO EXIT-PARAGRAPH with a PERFORM EXIT-PARAGRAPH.
Ugly.
```

>paragraph to the end of the PERFORM.

The THRU is not supposed to say how anything happens within the range, Mr 
Brazee; by design it transfers execution to the first statement following 
the specified label and returns execution to the statement following the 
PERFORM after the THRU condition is satisfied.

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-10T11:17:25-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8acdd32d-5fca-4c7c-9f4c-d6104fa83c79@d42g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<d2GPk.7270$cx5.2876@newsfe01.iad> <3890aac7-c251-4ac8-a2b9-4980735828c7@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <hkNRk.145893$cU2.30642@fe04.news.easynews.com> <77b33c53-a4c4-46e0-a472-b3d4caf8ed60@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <gf921i$2j3$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On Nov 10, 11:22 pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
> In article <77b33c53-a4c4-46e0-a472-b3d4caf8e...@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com>,
>
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> The range invoked by the THRU causes the code in Para3 to be executed,

That is completely incorrect.

Your statement implies that PERFORM Para1 THRU Para3 is the same as:
PERFORM Para1 PERFORM Para2 PERFORM Para3.

This is simple to prove. Write a program where the code is laid oout
as:

   PERFORM Para1 THRU Para3
   STOP RUN.
Para3.
   DISPLAY "3".
Para2.
   DISPLAY "2".
Para1.
   DISPLAY "1".

You will find that ""The range invoked by the THRU causes the code in
Para3 to be executed,"" is untrue.

Para3 will not be executed because it is not 'dropped into', (or go
toed )

The THRU _DOES NOT_ cause the paragraph to be executed. It is quite
the other way around: The executing and _falling_through_ of the
'thru' paragraph causes the PERFORM to be completed.

That is: the 'thru' paragraph must fall off the end. If there is a
PERFORM Para3 then this will not terminate the PERFORM Para1 THRU
Para3 even though the code in Para3 will execute.

> Mr
> Plinston... are you saying that range-execution is the same as falling
> through?

No, I am saying that 'range-execution' is not a useful way of imaging
what happens (see above). I am also saying that 'falling through' the
'THRU' paragraph is a requirement (ie the path of execution must go
beyond the last sentence in that paragraph *). I am also saying that
in a 'PERFORM Para1 THRU Para2' Para2 can only be entered and executed
by an explicit GOTO or by dropping into it from the paragraph above.

* NOTE: EXIT (by itself) is simply a noise word and is a no-op.


> If that is the case then how is the code which follows the PERFORM
> executed?

The PERFORM Para1 THRU Para3 is completed when the path of execution
falls off the end of the statements in Para3.

How hard is that ?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers

_(thread depth: 14)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<UG4Sk.167766$Xn4.140549@fe09.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<d2GPk.7270$cx5.2876@newsfe01.iad> <3890aac7-c251-4ac8-a2b9-4980735828c7@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <hkNRk.145893$cU2.30642@fe04.news.easynews.com> <77b33c53-a4c4-46e0-a472-b3d4caf8ed60@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <gf921i$2j3$1@reader1.panix.com> <8acdd32d-5fca-4c7c-9f4c-d6104fa83c79@d42g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard,
  Did you actually TRY this with any ANSI/ISO conforming compiler.

When you code
  Perform procedure-name-1 thru procedure-name-2

ALL conforming compilers *MUST* executeall statements in  proceudr-name-2

I could find the '85 Standard statement with a little work, but more easily (for 
me) from the '02 Standard,

GR(2) of the PERFORM statement,

" ... For an out-of-line PERFORM statement, the specified set of statements 
consists of all statements beginning with the first statement of 
procedure-name-1 and ending with the last statement of procedure-name-2, .."
```

```
On Nov 11, 1:52 pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.netcom.com>
wrote:
> Richard,
>   Did you actually TRY this with any ANSI/ISO conforming compiler.

Yes, of course I have, have you ?

In fact I recall back in the early 70s when we needed to do special
stuff with sorts we would do a go to out-of-line into a completely
different set of logic and when we wanted to do the actual sort we
would do a simple GO TO (end of sort input procedure) and then pick up
again after the SORT verb.


> When you code
>   Perform procedure-name-1 thru procedure-name-2
>
> ALL conforming compilers *MUST* executeall statements in  proceudr-name-2
>

Compilers do not 'execute' the code, that is the job of the run-unit
(or run-time).

But what that statement means is that the path of the code execution
must 'fall off' the end of procedure-name-2 because the point that
triggers the completion of the PERFORM lies beyond that last line but
before the next procedure.

For example:

    Procedure-Name-2.

       IF ( Never-Happens )
            DISPLAY "This is never executed"
       END-IF
       .

Then it does not 'execute all statements".


> I could find the '85 Standard statement with a little work, but more easily (for
> me) from the '02 Standard,
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Sure, it _must_ do that or abend in the attempt, or terminate without
abending because it falls of the end of the code without ever finding
procedure-name-2, or loop forever, or some other.

There is no 'detector' monitoring what is going on a deciding that it
needs to stop what is currently being calculated and do a quantum
phase change into procedure-name-2, it is up to the programmer to
ensure that it actually gets there by an explicit GO TO, ... or by
dropping into it from the paragraph above.

If you think that there is some other trigger that causes a return to
the PERFORM so that _it_ can "PERFORM procedure-name-2" then please
indicate where that is or how that could possibly work.



> --
> Bill Klein
…[92 more quoted lines elided]…
> > How hard is that ?
```

through to the next paragraph.

```
On Nov 11, 2:39 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[68 more quoted lines elided]…
> executed "falls thru" by the definition you are using.

The question asked by Robert used the term 'drop-into'. ie the way
that Para2 was entered was by dropping into it from above.

Alternately the label Para2 is 'dropped thru'.

Bill Klein already agreed that in his PERFORM Para1 THRU Para3 (with
Para2 between) that Para2 was dropped into, yet he proposed some other
unnamed mechanism for entering Para3 even though it was identical to
how Para2 was entered.

>
> Could you accept that this is maybe a matter of subjective interpretation,
> Richard? By YOUR definition of "fall thru" what you say is perfectly
> correct, but not everyone uses that definition.

The question was about 'drop-into'. That is: how does the path of
execution reach Para2. The answer is that is 'falls' downwards into
the Para2 and through it. How hard is that ?

In fact the return from a PERFORM, or PERFORM THRU is always from
_after_ the last statement in the scope of the range (even with an
EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION).

> Personally, I consider the scope of a PERFORM (Whether it is a PERFORMed
> paragraph, section or THRU ...) to be a defined scope  and what happens
> inside it is NOT "fall thru". I activated a scope explicitly. That is not by
> accident or causing a transfer of control I didn't want to happen.

The question was of whether the paragraph was 'dropped into' or had
some specific statement that took the control point there. For example
if Para1 contained a GO TO Para2 then that would be a specific
command.

PERFORM Para1 THRU Para2 is not the same as PERFORM Para1 PERFORM
Para2. In the latter case the second PERFORM is an explicit command to
Para2, in the former Para2, and all intermediates are simply 'dropped
into' and 'dropped thru'.

One may qualify 'dropped through' as being of a label (ie the
paragraph is 'dropped into') or of the code.

One thing is certain and that it with PERFORM Para1 THRU Para2 when
the end of Para1 is reached the code does _NOT_ ask the PERFORM what
to do next.


> (See earlier post in this thread)
>
> Pete.
> --
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers

_(thread depth: 16)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-11-10T22:29:38-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<hvshh4t0i29fae1l9sdsg279rd1u2eptj0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<e2OQk.69329$Fs5.13368@fe10.news.easynews.com> <3890aac7-c251-4ac8-a2b9-4980735828c7@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <hkNRk.145893$cU2.30642@fe04.news.easynews.com> <77b33c53-a4c4-46e0-a472-b3d4caf8ed60@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <6nqrt9FbquoU1@mid.individual.net> <3de11dc9-77bf-489e-b851-e9f1001d869e@e38g2000prn.googlegroups.com> <1d0hh418ut2m2608ihrgprgo79q0tm9rj8@4ax.com> <9835c134-fdae-48e9-87d3-32cb7e183688@q30g2000prq.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Mon, 10 Nov 2008 11:40:09 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>
>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>of the PERFORM makes it happen.

No, but the -exit paragraph SIMPLIFIES exiting. In the case of ..

    PERFORM Para1

Para1.
    ....
    ....
Para2.

.. when the program reaches the end of Para1, it must decide whether to return or fall
into Para2. That decision consumes time and energy. Perhaps it's only one joule, but
during an energy crisis with high gas prices, every little bit helps. For reference, one
drop of beer, as from an eyedropper, contains 100 joules of energy. Could YOU make 100
correct decisions on one drop?

In the simplified case of ..

    PERFORM Para1 THRU Para1-exit

Para1.
    ....
    ....
Para1-exit.
    EXIT.

Para2.

.. the compiler can tell from the similarity of names that Para1-exit is the end of Para1.
The fact that it contains only the word EXIT confirms its function. There is no
possibility of falling off Para1-exit into Para2, so no decision is necessary. Knowing the
return is unconditional, the compiler can omit the decision making code.

For those who favor ..

Para1.
Para2.
Para3.
Para1-exit. 

.. I would like to propose adding NEXT PARAGRAPH to the 2008 standard. It would be similar
in function to NEXT SENTENCE. I trust you'll support me on this.
```

- **References:** `<e2OQk.69329$Fs5.13368@fe10.news.easynews.com> <3890aac7-c251-4ac8-a2b9-4980735828c7@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <hkNRk.145893$cU2.30642@fe04.news.easynews.com> <77b33c53-a4c4-46e0-a472-b3d4caf8ed60@z6g2000pre.googlegroups.com> <6nqrt9FbquoU1@mid.individual.net> <3de11dc9-77bf-489e-b851-e9f1001d869e@e38g2000prn.googlegroups.com> <1d0hh418ut2m2608ihrgprgo79q0tm9rj8@4ax.com> <9835c134-fdae-48e9-87d3-32cb7e183688@q30g2000prq.googlegroups.com> <hvshh4t0i29fae1l9sdsg279rd1u2eptj0@4ax.com>`

```

Robert <no@e.mail> wrote in message
news:hvshh4t0i29fae1l9sdsg279rd1u2eptj0@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 10 Nov 2008 11:40:09 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
wrote:
>
> >Yes, you are right. It seems that most are being defensive about 'drop
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> .. when the program reaches the end of Para1, it must decide whether to
return or fall
> into Para2. That decision consumes time and energy. Perhaps it's only one
joule, but
> during an energy crisis with high gas prices, every little bit helps. For
reference, one
> drop of beer, as from an eyedropper, contains 100 joules of energy. Could
YOU make 100
> correct decisions on one drop?

Some irony or satire here, I think.


>
> In the simplified case of ..
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> .. the compiler can tell from the similarity of names that Para1-exit is
the end of Para1.
> The fact that it contains only the word EXIT confirms its function. There
is no
> possibility of falling off Para1-exit into Para2, so no decision is
necessary. Knowing the
> return is unconditional, the compiler can omit the decision making code.
>

I would be willing to bet a dollar that no compiler in existence is written
this way.  Similarity of paragraph names cannot be taken to be of any import
to the compiler.

While I don't know exactly how a PERFORM .AAA THRU AAA-X is implemented,
there must be code somewhere to determine how the program acts when it hits
the AAA-X, precisely because the compiler can't determine ahead of time what
the program flow has been.  As Richard keeps on pointing out there are
multiple ways it can get there.  Therefore, there must be code just before
each label to determine what to do - ignore the label (i.e., fall through)
or go somewhere else.

It looks like no more replies to my questions are going to come in so I'll
presently present a summary.

PL
```

```
On Tue, 11 Nov 2008 08:12:54 -0700, Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On Mon, 10 Nov 2008 23:20:09 -0600, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>through.

Micro Focus has a NOTRICKLE compiler option, which says the program will not fall out the
bottom of a paragraph. It cannot be used if the program contains -EXIT paragraphs.

If there are no GO TOs, no PERFORM THRU and no SECTIONs, paragraphs following an
unconditional EXIT PROGRAM, STOP RUN or GOBACK cannot fall out the bottom.
```

So those who are in favor of exit paragraphs still have a valid
argument.   In maintenance we don't want to redesign the flow, and
switches are, IMHO worse than GO TO EXIT.    

All languages need ways of exiting loops.   CoBOL programs need to be
able to exit performed paragraphs.   Right now, the options are clumsy
and subject to error.   Simplicity is the key here.

EXIT Paragraph
   and
EXIT Section

are used to "go to the exit point of the current paragraph/section

They are "analogous" to EXIT PERFORM

There is no statement to "exit the current performed range"

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message

Yes there is: GO TO range-exit. (Where range-exit is a single pargraph 
containing the word "EXIT." and is at the end of a SECTION or PERFORM 
...THRU...RANGE.)

Very effective, simple and efficient.

(Provided you implement code with the provisos already described at length 
here.)

I can't believe how this thread is dragging on, based on theoretical 
objections to something which, in practice, is not a problem...

I've never had a program go out of control when using my allegedly "archaic" 
coding style.:-)

I've asked several times for people to post cases where their production 
crashes are caused mainly by addressing exceptions (which WEREN'T down to 
bad subscripting/indexing) as opposed to DATA exceptions; no-one has... 
where is the problem, here?

Richard's point about "inheriting" a buch of code from some place where 
standards are unknown and you can't be sure the code doesn't contain runaway 
GO TOs is a valid one, but that is a scenario that is VERY infrequent for 
people still writing COBOL. The lack of automated tools to determine whether 
there is dodgy code or not is also valid, but no-one is likely to write such 
a tool because there is no demand for it in the real world, as far as COBOL 
goes.

The problems that COULD arise were sorted out decades ago by "Best 
Practices" and installation standards.

This is simply "Much Ado about Nothing", but not even half so well-written 
as Shakespeare's offering on the subject :-)

Pete.

```
On Nov 12, 8:50 am, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Tue, 11 Nov 2008 09:20:19 -0800 (PST), Richard
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> and subject to error.   Simplicity is the key here.


EXIT PARAGRAPH

EXIT SECTION

If you do an out-of-line PERFORM of a paragraph then EXIT PARAGRAPH
will terminate the PERFORM. Similar for section.

These are equivalent to in-line PERFORM's EXIT PERFORM CYCLE because a
PERFORM UNTIL will continue to the next cycle.

I dislike these because they are positional and have to be changed
when moving code between in-line and out-of-line. In any case it is
never difficult to construct code that doesn't need these escapes.


> --
> "In no part of the constitution is more wisdom to be found,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> - James Madison

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-14T13:01:35+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6o3tf2F1p9jbU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<3de11dc9-77bf-489e-b851-e9f1001d869e@e38g2000prn.googlegroups.com> <6nkkh49l63dtn3lvsjg6ndtlpe0r4553fo@4ax.com> <1sgmh4do7ciqmsev3jbc932gh416ao9qdl@4ax.com> <0l7nh4phuidd6j5d99iqneahosrrqtsudj@4ax.com> <gfhbad$o4h$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```


<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:gfhbad$o4h$1@reader1.panix.com...
> In article <0l7nh4phuidd6j5d99iqneahosrrqtsudj@4ax.com>,
> Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes, I remember posts to that effect (not from me, I hasten to add...) :-)

> As with anything new... what's the progression?  'First, 'It is
> impossible', next, 'It is heresy', finally 'Everyone knows that...''.

History in CLC (and the bulletin boards/forums which preceded it) has shown 
that is pretty much the case.

From my personal experience I can immediately think of two such instances:

1. Use of Relational Databases over flat files.
2. Object Oriented programming in COBOL.

I remember scorn and villification for suggesting there might be value in 
both of these things. Because I was younger then and not as wise and serene 
as I am now :-) I really tried to persuade people and I really cared that 
they didn't get it. It was a lot of wasted time and energy. (Well, maybe not 
wasted; at least I grew through it...now I don't care :-))

Nowadays, most people here are working with RDB as a matter of course, and 
more and more are recognising that Objects are playing a major part in the 
future of IT.

(This places Objects somewhere between the "heresy" and "Everyone knows..." 
levels of your scale.)

I'm watching with interest to see the first glimmers of enlightenment 
regarding component based systems, which are still very much "impossible" as 
far as most people here are concerned.... :-) (To be fair, you can't really 
get to it without going through OOP and recognising the importance of the 
paradigm, and that hasn't totally happened yet.)

I can also understand (now) that until people actually produce a component 
and realise it can plug into the network for remote access, or be embedded 
in a web page or a desktop application, it is pretty unreal for them. These 
same components can be the building blocks of both Web Services and SOA, so 
I suspect that as people begin to explore these options the concept will 
become more real. I look forward to the day when the use of components 
achieves "Everyone knows..." status, but I suspect that by the time it 
happens I'll no longer be posting here.


>
> (I also recall posting, a few years back, a mixture of IF... ELSE... IF...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> was writing.  If I can't find fame I guess I'll settle for infamy.)

"Infamy! Infamy! They've all got it in for me!"  (Kenneth Williams as Julius 
Caesar in "Carry On Cleo")

Style Wars are really pointless for the following reasons:

1. No-one's mind will be changed. (Instead heat gets generated because most 
people don't like being told that what they are doing or have done for 
decades is sub-optimum.)
2. There are fewer and fewer young COBOL newbies whose impressionable minds 
could be polluted by "Bad" COBOL style. Those who do find employment will be 
subject to installation standards and styles, whether those styles are 
"optimum" or not... (In very much the same way that first year Philosophy 
students measure everythng by Aristotle, they will adopt those standards as 
being the One True Way...)
3. Most of us evaluate style using different yardsticks. "Whatever they 
want...", "Whatever is easiest to write..." , "Whatever enables the code to 
be easily maintained...", "Whatever works and works well..."... (You may 
notice the use of "Whatever...". When it comes to style discussions, this is 
no coincidence. )
4. A truly professional programmer (in ANY language, including COBOL...) 
should rise above issues of style. Give me code in ANY style (including 
spaghetti...), and I'll fix, enhance, or alter it to do what is required. 
That's why I call myself a "programmer".... (Style may well have a bearing 
on the ease with which this is accomplished, but accomplished it will be, 
and style is only a passing consideration, not what programming is about.)

I can make this bold statement because I have actually done it. I've worked 
in this industry before there WAS "best practice" and "installation 
standards", and "peer reviews" and I did so on sites on different 
continents, using different COBOL platforms, with hugely varying corporate 
cultures (and sometimes even in foreign languages). If you call yourself a 
"programmer" you should be capable of programming, not bleating about the 
awful code someone left for you to fix at 2 in the morning... Is it COBOL? 
Yes. Are you a COBOL programmer? Yes. Well bloody get on with it then...:-)

I get tired very quickly of people who want to be "heroes" because they did 
what they are being paid for. So, you had to fix crap code...if the code 
wasn't crap you wouldn't have to fix it. Ever thought about that?  Be glad 
there is crap code; it leads to chances of employment.

If you discard and denigrate code that works because you don't like the 
style it is written in, you are NOT a programmer; rather, a persnickety, 
punctillious, pedantic, programming pussy, who should not be accorded the 
honourable job title of "programmer". Think about getting a job in the Civil 
Service where pussies can hide behind procedure, or maybe a career in 
politics might suit better?

Anyway, sun is shining, it is 24 degrees and I'm off to the beach... :-)

I'll try not to think about COBOL programming styles while I enjoy the 
surf... :-)

Pete.
```

_(thread depth: 27)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-15T13:47:50+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6o6khnF25936U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<3de11dc9-77bf-489e-b851-e9f1001d869e@e38g2000prn.googlegroups.com> <6nkkh49l63dtn3lvsjg6ndtlpe0r4553fo@4ax.com> <1sgmh4do7ciqmsev3jbc932gh416ao9qdl@4ax.com> <0l7nh4phuidd6j5d99iqneahosrrqtsudj@4ax.com> <gfhbad$o4h$1@reader1.panix.com> <6o3tf2F1p9jbU1@mid.individual.net> <s47rh4l04hvh7d3kv2nbkhc4g7semt1rlk@4ax.com> <6o5mo9F1vr3nU1@mid.individual.net>`

```


"Bill Gunshannon" <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote in message 
news:6o5mo9F1vr3nU1@mid.individual.net...
> In article <s47rh4l04hvh7d3kv2nbkhc4g7semt1rlk@4ax.com>,
> Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> writes:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> Same here.

I don't recall you being a regular poster here, at the time I am talking 
about, Bill.

It's easy with hindsight to believe there was only a gradual transition. 
There was, but some of the "early adopters" were completely rubbished for 
even suggesting there might be an advantage in having an open, rather than a 
closed file system. (I still believe that one of the nails in COBOL's coffin 
was the closed nature of the file system. Given a choice betweeen going cap 
in hand to an often supercilious IT department when you need a new report 
(the data can only be accessed by a COBOL program), or simply calling it off 
yourself from a relational database, the choice was a no brainer. Business 
voted with its feet and the result is what we see today. COBOL HAD to 
support RDB just to "keep up"...:-))

In fact, IBM released DB2 in 1982 or 3 (I can't remember exactly and I can't 
be bothered to go and look it up...I was on the second course for DB2 which 
was run by IBM in Reading, Berkshire.) This was coincidentally the same time 
as the release of the IBM PC.  Relational databases and DB2 in particular 
were strongly resisted by most IBM mainframe installations. There were some 
very zealous converts (usually specialist IBMers) who went around 
evangelizing the new data technology, but all they really succeeded in doing 
was alienating everybody and further entrenching resistance. This, coupled 
with some quite serious perfomance problems in the early editions led to the 
uptake being much less than IBM had hoped for. It was not until the early 
'90s that Relational technology began to be accepted on mainframe sites. By 
1997, (with the advent of Office '97 for Windows) people could easily build 
their own databases and did so. This spread to the workplace and saw the IT 
department's stranglehold on the corporate data asset start to be loosened. 
It went too far and the result was pretty much chaos without any real 
control over data. Today there is a balance between local reporting and data 
needs, which are the responsibility of the local office/department manager, 
and the corporate backbone data repository managed by the IT department. 
(Very few of these repositories, to my knowledge, are still using flat 
files...)

So, yes, there was a "gradual" transition throughout the industry as a 
whole, but there was also an entrenched mindset in many quarters which 
fiercely resisted this transition.

I copped a lot of flak here for suggesting that people using PC COBOL should 
move from ISAM to RDB, based on one being an open system and the other being 
closed.

>
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> very happy with.

Those xray specs you bought off the back of a Marvel comic are actually an 
illusion... :-)

The Emperor is not only fully clothed, but his Empire is overwhelming yours 
on a daily basis.
>
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> to many people's satisfaction.

OO share of the world's IT market:  94%

COBOL and other procedural paradigm languages: 6%

OO programmers in the world:  Around 190 million (includes hobbyists and 
"after school" people)

COBOL programmers in the world: Maybe 5 million (includes Asia and the 
sub-continent...)

(Figures based on suspect Internet data - but probably true (ish)...)

Define "many"...:-)

Pete.
```

On mainframes the 'Cobol files' are just files, anything can access
them. On many machines that was also true. On ICL DRS20 micros the
files could be accessed by Cobol, Pascal, Basic.

Microfocus supported C-ISAM so that any language can also access the
files just by using the C-ISAM package - this was part of the X-Open
standard. Infomix sits (or used to) on top of C-ISAM files giving
'open' access to them.

Many systems have ODBC access to the Cobol files.

You should note that relational databases are not just one thing and
that they all have different underlying data structures are all
accessed by different mechanisms and all have different extensions
which may ensure that incompatabilities abound.

Sure, just use ODBC. You can do that to Cobol files.


> Business
> voted with its feet and the result is what we see today. COBOL HAD to
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> closed.

MS Access is not 'open' in any sense that could not be claimed for C-
ISAM.

Granted one can now get truly open SQL DB systems such as MySQL and
PostgreSQL. But what were you suggesting: that PC based Cobol
developers should shell out a few hundred thousand for Oracle ?

Many Cobols also supported BTrieve which was a centralised isam file
system on Novel servers with XDB giving reporting and even allowing
ODBC access.

BTrieve could have been used with existing programs with almost no
changes just by setting the compiler options (and buying btrieve).

Fujitsu files are supported directly by such things as Crystal
Reports.


> >> I've still seen nothing to indicate that OO CoBOL is anything except
> >> marginal.   OO and CoBOL have seemed to have gone in separate
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Probably based on the fact that, say, C++ _can_ use classes (or can be
used as pure C) so every C/C++ programmer therefore is fully OO.  Same
with VB, PERL, and many others.

It is likely that because there is OO available in Cobol that every
Cobol programmer, on the basis above, should be classed as OO.

programmer. My point was about NON-TECHNICAL users.

Because non-technical people were starting to use databases like Access and 
Excel to exchange information, it became clear to me that if COBOL did not 
support this it would be left behind. I advocated that programmers using 
COBOL on PCs should consider moving to RDB so that core systems could be 
part of the "data liberation" I saw occurring on a number of sites where I 
worked. I also saw at first hand the frustration experienced by users who 
had to request that a COBOL program be written every time they needed access 
to what was, in fact, their own data.

In this forum I received little but scorn for my suggestion. It really 
doesn't matter now (in fact, most of the people I remember objecting have 
either stopped contibuting here or now use RDBs as a matter of course in 
their daily work), but it DOES support Doc's original, mostly accurate, if 
somewhat ascerbic, observation.

(The real irony is that I am now phasing out embedded SQL as I believe it to 
be obsolete... wheels turn, circles become complete... and so on.)

Pete.

On Nov 15, 11:28 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[109 more quoted lines elided]…
> to what was, in fact, their own data.

In my experience of users doing their own thing, which goes back to
Osborne luggables being programmed in Basic by users, or using
SuperCalc and dBaseII, is that user generated programs, spreadsheets
and databases have little to do with the data that is processed by the
main applications.

They do not write up a spreadsheet that mimics what the business
already does because it is too difficult to get a report written, they
do it because it is completely different data.

Whether the main business systems use ISAM or RDB is quite irrelevant,
the users would still poke around using Excel or similar because
creating their own databases or tables, or modifying main application
ones would bring down the wrath of the database administrator.

As it happens in one of my corporate clients I do much of my work by
taking some user built spreadsheets, as they are discovered, and
turning them into new systems that can be used corporate wide.

The main problem in doing this is that it is trying to formalise what
is basically arbitrary. Having the users build spreadsheets at least
gets them to clarify their requirements even if it is by trial and
error.

Having the main application data in RDBs (which it actually is anyway)
would make no difference to the users that I work with.


> In this forum I received little but scorn for my suggestion. It really
> doesn't matter now (in fact, most of the people I remember objecting have
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

Mr Dashwood, to resist a new technology which has 'quite serious

incurs substantial investments in software, hardware and personnel (folks

industry regulations, local, state and Federal laws all while staying 
within the budget you've been granted (after much argument and 
second-guessing) might cause those folks with Say Over The Matter to be a 
bit... conservative.

On Sat, 15 Nov 2008 13:47:50 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>It's easy with hindsight to believe there was only a gradual transition. 
>There was, but some of the "early adopters" were completely rubbished for 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>support RDB just to "keep up"...:-))

I don't call it a closed file system when I program with the same file
systems that every other language and application on a computer uses.

I know PCs don't have indexed and relative file systems, which CoBOL
expects, so PC CoBOL has to create their own.   But mainframes and
minis of various makes have not had this limitation.

Is my point really so obtuse that people here can't get it, or is it just

Someone using a computer system who is NOT a programmer just wants to be 
able to manipulate data. They neither know nor care about learning and using 
a computer programming language like COBOL. If their data has been locked 
into a file system which requires a COBOL program to access it, then it is, 
to all intents and purposes, "closed" for them.

This was the situation at the time I was writing about. I would agree that 
today it is a bit better, largely due to better availability of ODBC and 
user friendly front ends like Crystal Reports and similar.

Pete.

You seem to be very flexible in your memory of 'then', and very
unspecific about when 'then' was.

I have already pointed out that ODBC was available for Cobol files in
1995. Crystal Reports was availble on Fujitsu's PC Cobol only slightly
later. At that time most PCs were 486s running Windows 3.1 or perhaps
a P133 with Windows 95.

The RDBs of that time: Infomix, Oracle, DB2 required deprartmental
servers and the price of a house to buy and run them, and then there
were annual support fees.

So why would PC developers (the ones you advocated use RDBs) change to
using a mechanism that would double their product price and cost more
than their income to support.

Today the smallest and cheapest machine that can be be bought can run
PostgreSQL for free, even my handheld Nokia could run MySQL (granted
only version 4) and I program for it using SQLite.

So instead of just saying 'then' put down some date and specify which
RDB they would have run on their 8088/80386/.. DOS 3.3/Win3.1/Win95/..
system that would have been: a) cost effective, b) sufficiently fast,
c) saleable to their client base.



>

My comment is a result of failure by responses to address the point I was 
trying to make.

Here's a recap and the last one I'm posting:

1. Doc Dwarf suggests 3 stages of reaction to new ideas. (I don't think it 
was entirely serious, but I responded in kind)

2. I agree with Doc's three stages and cite a couple of instances from my 
own experience.
    One of these was a strong reaction against my suggesting here that RDB 
might be better for COBOL than continued use of ISAM files.
    (The main reason why I believed this was because I was working on sites 
where I saw a growing reaction from users about needing to have  COBOL 
programs written whenever they required access to data. Whether RDB software 
was available or not, users were just beginning to cotton on to it.  (You 
took extreme umbrage because I suggested this occurred at around the same 
time as Access 97 became available. It was an observation, not a value 
judgement. But you consistently seem to overreact when Microsoft is 
mentioned...))

3. My "point" was that if the core systems were using a RDB then the data 
could be accessed in a standard way using standard tools and interfaces with 
which people were becoming familiar. In this way, there would be no need for 
users to be anti-COBOL.

I no longer wish to defend this in present time because circumstances have 
changed and RDB is pretty much the norm now. That was then and this is now.

There is no "put down" and I have nothing but respect for Howard and 
yourself, as people and as programmers.

I'm deleting the rest of your post because my point is not about when RDBs 
were available.

I already explained fully the circumstances under which I believed core 
systems should use RDBs and why that meant that COBOL (as the major provider 
of core systems) should move to RDB.

I really have nothing more to say on this that I haven't said already and, 
coupled with that, I find your posts difficult to respond to because there 
is no chevronning of quoted material in them (so it is not easy to identify 
what you said and what I am commenting on), notwithstanding the hostile tone 
and spinning which doesn't predispose me to respond anyway.

Pete.

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:6oe0o6F3674aU1@mid.individual.net...
>
<much snippage>
> You are a programmer.
>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Pete,
  Most of the LARGE IBM mainframe shops that I worked with in the 80's and early 
90's had "ad hoc" report software that could be run from "limited access" TSO 
(ISPF) sessions.  There were complex systems like SAS and (almost) scripting 
languages like SPITBOL.  The things that these had in common were:

1) "end users" could create "ad hoc" requests
2) "end users" could do this from an ISPF-look-alike screen (which was the 
"standard" in those environments)
3) Almost universally these were "reporting" systems and not "update" systems

The fact that IBM "file systems" were universally available in so many shops 
made it profitable for vendors to create such programs and users to know how to 
use them.

When PC's became more common, there were also "secure" extraction products that 
allowed end users to "pull" their specific data for manipulation on their own 
PCs.  About that time, DB2 was taking over as a common data storage system and 
QMF and other tools were available for that

HOWEVER, I will gladly admit that such software was not universal, so many 
coming from an IBM mainframe environment may not have had the experiences that I 
did.
```

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:wGrUk.75492$mZ6.27990@fe07.news.easynews.com...
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
> news:6oe0o6F3674aU1@mid.individual.net...
…[47 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Thank you for responding to my point, Bill.

I remember teaching users (on IBM sites) QBE and even a little SPUFI. But 
there was no general facility for training users in these tools, provided by 
the IT department.

So I don't disagree with your post.

My real concern was what I heard from interaction with users. I've always 
believed that IT is a service and taken pride in providing that service to 
the business. I'm sure there are many here who feel the same. It isn't all 
about IT. What I was hearing was serious dissatisfaction with the service 
provided by IT. Typically, user departments could wait for weeks to get a 
COBOL program, and this was not just on 1 site but a number of them (all in 
Europe). As easy access tools became available and people started using them 
it became highly likely that people were going to bypass IT and do their own 
thing. This was what originally brought me to the conclusion that COBOL 
needed to be more "open" and perceived to be so. If that didn't happen, 
there wouldn't be a place for it.

I think history proved that my fears were justified. Once people had an 
alternative they were much more prepared to do their own thing. Even as 
recently as two years ago on a major site in Auckland, one of the "problems" 
faced by IT was very intelligent users (some with degrees in computer 
science) building elaborate spreadsheets and databases that were highly 
complex and were not feeding the corporate backbone properly. It took some 
very delicate negotiation to get these people to agree to work WITH IT 
instead of outside it.

(This site was using COBOL and Java on Unisys and IBM servers. With 
Microsoft clients and workstations. The general perception of users was that 
things got done reasonably quickly in Java but not in COBOL. (COBOL was very 
much a mystery to most users and seen as necessary for legacy, but not for 
anything else.) Most of the user departments were avoiding contact with the 
IT department and doing their own thing. A new IT director brought some 
serious change (for the better) and the last I heard they were moving to SOA 
with much more user involvement. All data was stored on RDBs for easy shared 
access.

The real context of my post was in support of Doc's tongue-in-cheek 
contention about reaction to new technology. I really didn't want to argue 
the whole thing again; it was a long time ago and we all know better now.

Pete.
```

X"tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:H%CUk.2266$jV1.1525@newsfe13.iad...
>
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> straightforward reports it's a doddle in RPG.

I think that most of the sites I've  worked on where they used COBOL did NOT 
encourage RPG or even have it.

I agree it is a useful tool and I have seen people who are skilled in it do 
quite amazing things in a very short period of time. The same can be said 
for Easytrieve.

However, it is VERY easy for users to share categorized information using 
desktop spreadsheets and databases and they don't need to get IT involved, 
which was a major attraction for many of them.

I think there was a kind of realization that, with the advent of standard 
desktop tools on corporate workstations, users now had an option. They could 
store and organize their own data and flick it over the network to other 
departments, without needing to request programming from IT.

As they became more adept at doing this, the information locked in the IT 
departments flat files became less relevant to them. (In effect, It was 
being sidelined by the user base...)

The major advantages of using RDB with COBOL which I saw at that time were:

1. IT could participate in the "data revolution" that was happening and not 
get sidelined by it.
2. COBOL would be perceived by the user base as something IT used, to 
provide data they could access easily  and share, not some esoteric language 
used by IT to lock data away from them. (Perception is often just as 
important as actual technology...)
3. Moving core IT systems to RDB would make users less suspicious of what IT 
were doing and encourage them to contribute their own data feeds to the 
corporate repository. It is much easier to persuade a user department to 
provide a weekly feed when they are familiar with what you are talking about 
and understand why and how the data is required. If you can offer them some 
support at the same time you can build a good relationship between IT and 
the user base.

Now, many people reading this will say," We always have a good relationship 
with our user base.". Good for you. Sadly, I have worked on sites where this 
was not the case and IT were perceived as being sorcerers working spells in 
their own ivory towers and NOT  being responsive to requests from the user 
base. Typically a simple report could take two weeks, a new subsystem 
months, even years.

The problem was that IT had their own (usually technology based) vision of 
where they were going with corporate systems.  User departments did not feel 
involved or engaged.  (They were briefly involved during requirements 
gathering. After that it was just waiting, constant slippage, and getting an 
end product which was no longer entirely relevant to their needs, was 
costing them an arm and a leg, and which they had no choice over but "take 
it or leave it". If the advent of PCs did nothing else, it encouraged users 
to get involved in IT. Today, things are much better in this regard. SOA 
encourages everyone to work together defining useful services and fuelling 
them, but this is not the only way.)

This is partly why I became converted to the idea of interactive, iterative 
development, with user involvement throughout the development life cycle. 
And not just token meetings with them, but actual JAD workshops where stuff 
gets protoyped at the time, and new objects are identified and modelled 
against existing ones. They become an essential part of the process, and 
the end result is as much theirs as it is ITs. They feel like they own it. 
As such, they are more "forgiving" if it isn't perfect immediately, and will 
work to correct teething problems in the next iteration. (Oddly enough, 
systems developed in this way seem to have fewer problems anyway, teething 
or otherwise. Instead of: "It's very nice, but can we just have this one 
small change...?" You get: "Oh Man, this is exactly what we need right 
now...!" (Actual quotes, memorised from my own experience...))

(Although I don't like to call my approach "Agile", it is certainly more 
acrobatic than the Waterfall... :-)) Whatever you call it, it works very 
well.

>
> (I gained a lifelong friend and advocate with one of these, quite by
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Yes, little things do mean a lot to users. The fact that you were responsive 
and professional cuts a lot of ice with them. They are simply not used to it 
in many cases... :-)

Pete.
```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message

OO COBOL IS marginal (now). I agree.

(It's really a pity... OO COBOL , at least in the Fujitsu implementation (I 
haven't used it with MF) is a superb product. I used it for many years and 
it steered me towards components (which I believe are the Keys to the 
Kingdom... :-)). Sadly, as greater understaning of OO programming is 
acquired, OO COBOL seems "clunky" and verbose, compared to other leaner OO 
languages. I believe it is to do with the requirement to maintain source 
code which was always a prime consideration for COBOL.)

However, it is a stepping stone for COBOL people towards objects, and I 
predict we will see more use of it as COBOL apps are migrated away from the 
procedural paradigm.

I was interested to note that both Micro Focus and Fujitsu keep a low 
profile on it in their white papers, but both endorse it once COBOL is 
running on the network.

For automated migration tools, it is important to be able to wrap existing 
COBOL as objects, so OO COBOL is pretty indispensable.


>
> Which I don't mind.   Why does the best tool of the old paradigm need
> to be adjusted to the new paradigm when there are plenty of new tools
> designed for it?

It is because a transition between the two paradigms is necessary, unless 
you are prepared to throw away all your current investment and start over. 
OO COBOL is an important step towards that transition. You COULD continue 
development in OO COBOL and have your applications run in modern 
environments that are Object Oriented (like .NET/Mono), but most programmers 
will eventually "bite the bullet" and move to more productive OO languages.

>
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> My mind got changed - didn't yours?

:-) No, and I don't believe yours did either. (I did accept that some of the 
arguments are very good and worth considerng. As I no lnger develop in COBOL 
it was not really important to me.) What specific arguments in the 
discussions on styles that have raged here over the last decade, caused you 
to change your COBOL coding style :-)?

Pete.

   OBTAIN CALC IAARCAA                  
       ON DB-REC-NOT-FOUND              
           PERFORM 1040-NO-IAARCAA      
       END-IF.                          

That made doubly sure that the implicit IF that the pre-compiler put
in was obviously finished.

```
On Tue, 11 Nov 2008 08:10:52 -0700, Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On Mon, 10 Nov 2008 22:29:38 -0500, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:
>
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
>needs to have code to account for that possibility.   

What happens when two performs have a common exit? Consider:

 main. 
    perform section-a


 section-a section. 
 a.
   ....
   perform a-x
   ...
 a-x.
     continue.
D   display 'exiting'.

 section-b section.
D    display 'Please help me, I'm falling'.

One would expect this to be covered by the Standard, but it's not. What happens depends on
the compiler.

The Micro Focus Way
    The exit to main is destroyed by the second perform of a-x. The section does not exit,
execution falls into section-b. But wait, it gets better. If a-x were changed to an empty
paragraph, by turning off debug, the optimizer would have discarded perform a-x, which
would make it return as expected. 

The Ryan-McFarland Way
    Almost the same except section exits are distinct from paragraph exits. In this case,
it works as expected. If main had said perform a thru a-x, the second trip through a-x
would have fallen into section-b.

The Realia Way
    Works as expected. Returns are pushed on the stack. 

The Mainframe Way
    Works as expected, HOWEVER exits can only be nested TWO DEEP.  A third perform of a-x
would have destroyed the return to main. Try to figure that one out at 2am.

>>For those who favor ..
>>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>functionality you are proposing.

You mean EXIT PARAGRAPH. Those who PERFORM THRU would object to the semantics, which imply
exiting from the perform when it is actually going to the next paragraph.
```

```
On Tue, 11 Nov 2008 20:42:57 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 12, 4:49ï¿½pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Tue, 11 Nov 2008 08:10:52 -0700, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
>since at least as far back as '74, probably '68 or even earlier.

(14.5.9, Overlapping operands)
37) PERFORM statement. The results of the execution of overlapping PERFORM statements are
undefined.

(14.8.27, PERFORM statement, general rule 11)
11) The results of executing the following sequence of PERFORM statements are undefined
and no exception
condition is set to exist when the sequence is executed:
a) a PERFORM statement is executed and has not yet terminated, then
b) within the range of that PERFORM statement another PERFORM statement is executed, then
c) the execution of the second PERFORM statement passes through the exit of the first
PERFORM statement.
NOTE Because this is undefined, the user should avoid such an execution sequence. On some
implementations
it causes stack overflows, on some it causes returns to unlikely places, and on others
other actions can occur.
Therefore, the results are unpredictable and are unlikely to be portable.

>> The Micro Focus Way
>> ï¿½ ï¿½ The exit to main is destroyed by the second perform of a-x. The section does not exit,
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>some of the limitations of the standard. See PERFORM-TYPE directive.

PERFORM-TYPE 

MF  Only the exit point of the innermost PERFORM currently being executed is recognized
and its return jump taken.

OSVS The exit point of any PERFORM statement currently being executed is recognized if
reached; the return jump taken is the first reached. PERFORM statements with the same exit
point can be nested to a depth of two (one inner and one outer). If they are nested
deeper, they do not return correctly. The end of a section is regarded as the same point
as the end of its last paragraph.

PERFORM-TYPE"OSVS" provides compatibility with the mainframe behavior of OS/VS COBOL,
DOS/VS COBOL, VS COBOL II and COBOL/370.

RM The exit point of any PERFORM statement currently being executed is recognized if
reached; the return jump taken is the first reached. PERFORM statements with the same exit
point cannot be nested; if they are they do not return correctly. The end of a section is
regarded as a separate point from the end of its last paragraph.


>Microfocus copes fine with your code and does the performs correctly
>without dropping out as you claim.
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
```

limit is reached. This error code existed all the way back to MF's CIS
Cobol of 1978 where it was mentioned that there were 22 levels of
perform stack available.

```
Richard,
  You keep talking about Robert's note on "drop-into".

*MY* note in this thread was in specific reply to your note of
    Thursday, November 06, 2008 2:58 PM

which was in reply to a note from
      Bill Gunshannon

in which you make the statement (Bill's comments come first),

"> PERFORM COUNT-PARA THRU COUNT-EXIT.
>
> COUNT-PARA.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Both Paragraphs are explicitly mentioned. No GOTO. No Fall-thru.

You misunderstand what 'fall into', or fall thru, means.

The actual answer, based on your example, is b. always fall into.

That is, after the ADD 1 the next thing that happens is that the
program 'falls into' the Count-Exit paragraph.

In fact the PERFORM a THRU z specifies exactly that the program should
start at a and (fall) thru until the end of z.
 >>> end quoted text.

I then pointed out that the Standard allows

Perform Para2 thru Para1
   with a structure of

Para1.
   ...
Para2.
   ...

And you never really answered how your logic of what PERFMR THRU does applies to 
this valid structure.

My understand was and remains that both of the procedure-names in a PERFORM THRU 
statement are explicitly referenced in the PERFORM statement and there is no 
"fall thru/into" for either of them.

Pete indicated that in his view, there isn't a "fall into/thru" for procedures 
in the "logical structure" of the PERFORM THRU.  I have come across that view in 
the past (unlike your/Richard's view) and would certainly accept it.  My 
personal view "leans" toward saying that unnamed procedures in the PERFORM THRU 
structure *may* be considered as "fall into/thru".  I simply reject that any 
epxlicitly referenced procedure-name from the PERFORM THRU statement is handled 
by a "fall into/thru".

I do, however, also accept that this is a "what do you mean by term-xyz" issue 
and really does NOT impact programming or debugging - in any cases that I am 
aware of.  It may (or may not) be reflected in the individual or "shop" 
standards for programming, but what you call this should NOT change the style 
used (IMHO).
```

```
On Nov 11, 1:42 pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.netcom.com>
wrote:
> Richard,
>   You keep talking about Robert's note on "drop-into".
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
> this valid structure.

Actually you _DIDN'T_ ask that, though you probably intended to. You
asked:

> Perform Para2 thru Para1
>    ...
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>    Display "first".

Which is why I told you to re-read your message. If you had you
probably would have made it meaningful at that time.

As to what my answer is, it is exactly as I stated: It will 'PERFORM
(starting at) Para2 (until it falls) THRU (to the end of) Para1. As it
never finds the end of Para1, given there is no goto to get back
there, the code path will fall off the end of the program and this
will 'abend' or similar without ever completing the PERFORM.

What would you expect ?  It certainly will _not_ "perform para1
because it is mentioned in the THRU" clause.


> My understand was and remains that both of the procedure-names in a PERFORM THRU
> statement are explicitly referenced in the PERFORM statement and there is no
> "fall thru/into" for either of them.

So what are you saying ? When the end of the program code is reached
while dropping into every paragraph after Para2 in the search for the
end of Para1 then Para1 will be executed and the PERFORM terminates ?

Of will it jump immediately to Para1 when it reaches the end of Para2
and will ignore everything after Para2 ?

Why don't you try it then ?

You may learn why your understanding is wrong.


> Pete indicated that in his view, there isn't a "fall into/thru" for procedures
> in the "logical structure" of the PERFORM THRU.

What is each label then ? A brick wall that is impenitrable ? Does the
program go into a quantum state that arrives at the first line of the
next paragraph from some other direction ?


But even you agreesd that in "PERFORM Para1 THRU Para3" Para2 was
dropped into, but claimed that somehow Para3 was not.

Para1.
   blah blah.
    |   <- drop into Para2
    v
Para2.
   blah blah.
    |   <- NOT drop into Para3 ??? something else ?
    v
Para3.
   blah blah.
   much other code.
  (implicit return to after PERFORM statement)-^

It is supposed to go back to the PERFORM at the end of Para2, or
Para1, and get directions to do Para3 ???

Does that actually happen when you test out variations of this code ?

Have you ever tried it ?

How you looked at the actual machine code to see what actually
happens ?

>  I have come across that view in
> the past (unlike your/Richard's view) and would certainly accept it.  My
> personal view "leans" toward saying that unnamed procedures in the PERFORM THRU

What is an 'unnamed procedure' ? In Cobol a procedure is defined as
being "one paragraph, a set of several consecutive paragraphs, or ..."

A paragraph must have a label. How do you get a 'procedure' with no
label naming it ?

> structure *may* be considered as "fall into/thru".

Perhaps you meant 'unreferenced' names.

> I simply reject that any
> epxlicitly referenced procedure-name from the PERFORM THRU statement is handled
> by a "fall into/thru".

The THRU simply sets the end point, it does not and cannot make that
code execute. It is up to the programmer to ensure that the end point
is reached in order to terminate the PERFORM.

Your view is inverted, and wrong. There is a vast difference between
"PERFORM Para1 THRU Para2" and "PERFORM Para1 PERFORM Para2".

In fact Cobol behaves more like "PERFORM (starting at) Para1 UNTIL
(the end of) Para2", and just like 'UNTIL X > 100' there is no
guarantee that this will ever be satisfied, it is up to the programmer
to ensure that it will be (by dropping into it or gotoing it).


> I do, however, also accept that this is a "what do you mean by term-xyz" issue
> and really does NOT impact programming or debugging -

It certainly does if you think (wrongly) that "PERFORM Para1 THRU
Para2" and "PERFORM Para1 PERFORM Para2" are the same thing. Or even
that the THRU is treated by the compiler like an 88 level 'VALUE "A"
THRU "Z"' where, perhaps, the PERFORM is expanded to a list of the
paragraphs between.


> in any cases that I am
> aware of.  It may (or may not) be reflected in the individual or "shop"
> standards for programming, but what you call this should NOT change the style
> used (IMHO).


However when they state that the THRU paragraph will be executed
_because_ it is mentioned in the PERFORM then it shows a
misunderstanding of Cobol, and thus a need to peer review more
agressively.



> Bill Klein
>  wmklein <at> ix.netcom.com"Richard" <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[123 more quoted lines elided]…
> >> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

See my other note.

In
  perform procedure-name-1 thru procedure-name-2

then procedure-name-2 *IS* performed by any ANSI/ISO conforming compiler.  This 
is true no matter what order procedure-name-1 and procedure-name-2 appear in the 
source code.

```
My sincere apologies to Richard and my clear statement that I was in error (at 
least with one compiler).  I am still testing this with other compilers.

With at least one compiler, if you have

 Perform Para1 thru Para2

and Para2 is physically before Para1 in the source code, then with this 
compiler, you do NOT get to Para2 UNLESS an explicit GO TO (or some other 
explicit transfer of control) occurs.

As I say, this is NOT my understanding of the Standard and I am still testing 
this, but my initial indication is that Richard's understanding is correct and 
that mine was in error.
```

Bill,

I don't use PERFORM THRU unless I am maintaining other code, but I 
always assumed when someone coded PERFORM PARAGRAPH-A THRU PARAGRAPH-B 
that control would be transferred to PARAGRAPH-A and control would be 
returned only after reaching the end of PARAGRAPH-B.

agree with Richard that you have to get to PARAGRAPH-B either by an 
explicit GO TO PARAGRAPH-B or by falling into it from a previous

>On Nov 11, 6:06ï¿½pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.netcom.com>

>the code' ?

Getting to para2 is the programmer's responsibility, not the oompiler's. If source code
does not send control to para2, the source code is not ANSI/ISO compliant.

14.8.27.3
"Statements such as the GO TO statement, the PERFORM statement, and the procedure format
of the EXIT statement may occur in the flow of execution of the specified set of
statements, however the flow of execution should eventually pass to the end of
procedure-name-2."
```

```
Robert,
  You were confused by not knowing "official Standard-speak"

The word "should" in
    "... however the flow of execution should eventually pass to the end of > 
procedure-name-2."

does NOT mean that source code that violates this "recommendation" is not 
conforming.

See:
   Table 1 - Verbal forms
on page 21 of the '02 Standard.

For should it has

 "a recommendation expressed by the standard; it need not be followed"
   and
"it is recommended that"
```

- **Message-ID:** `<6nqr79FbomlU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<d2GPk.7270$cx5.2876@newsfe01.iad> <vvv0h45okn0ta3afojtnh66l6cd9crh1un@4ax.com> <fj12h4pe2rhi2veb7ibgop6i4l319vdd8a@4ax.com> <6ndi1eFkoho1U1@mid.individual.net> <5mg3h4h2jp2irqhf449n9531c0ls00pf6s@4ax.com> <5lk3h4d55e67bsvkftdtfqps3dmvip3tgj@4ax.com> <6ne34vFl6rj3U1@mid.individual.net> <7d19d384-fefa-44dc-ab59-b0319919f08b@s9g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <e2OQk.69329$Fs5.13368@fe10.news.easynews.com> <3890aac7-c251-4ac8-a2b9-4980735828c7@i18g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <hkNRk.145893$cU2.30642@fe04.news.easynews.com>`

```


"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:hkNRk.145893$cU2.30642@fe04.news.easynews.com...
> Richard,
>  Not worth responding in detail.  You use of "fall-thru" is NOT the same 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> the explicit PERFORM statement.

I agree with Bill's interpretation and I'd go further... I'd say that in the 
example above the execution of para2 is NOT a "fall thru" because it is part 
of the specified scope of the PERFORM.

If I PERFORM a SECTION with several paragraphs in it (provided I don't do 
any branching out of the section) then that SECTION is a defined scope and 
it is arguable if paragraphs receiving control within that scope, are "fall 
thrus". (More importantly, it is irrelevant if I am looking to debug a 
roblem with control flow...)

For example:

some section.
p000.

.....

p010.

....

p020.

    if some-condition
       go to p000
    end-if
...

p999.
    exit.
===============================

It seems pointless to me to argue whether control "falls thru"  each of the 
paragraphs... it doesn't matter. The defined scope is the SECTION and that 
is the "unit" of control transference that we need to be concerned with.

Similarly, PERFORM  para1 thru para3 defines an explicitly scoped "unit" of 
control transference, in fact, the scope of any PERFORM does this. What 
happens inside the scope of that unit is of no consequence; problems of 
control flow occur only when we jump out of this defined scope, or 
inadvertently fall out of it, and that cannot happen if it has been 
PERFORMed (and there are no GO TOs or ALTERED procedures within the scope.)

It really comes down to what you mean by "fall thru".

You can argue that all code execution is a "fall thru" to the next 
sequential instruction, unless diverted by a branch or jump in which case 
you have simply "fallen" into a different location... But what point does 
such an argument serve?

Like I said before, this whole discussion would be important if control flow 
was problematic, but for the most part, it isn't. Checks and balances 
already in place on most installations, plus programmer experience over 
years, all combine to make it simply a storm in a teacup. Peter's survey 
showed that at least the people who responded, all applied constraints to 
code to prevent control flow issues (Minimal use of GO TO, one entry and one 
exit to a performed scope, no ALTERs, etc.),  most production problems are 
caused by bad data or data that is outside the validation scope of the 
program concerned, NOT with control flow branching out of a PERFORMED range, 
or dropping inot places where it shouldn't.

When's the last time you saw an endless loop in production, or S0C3/4 (NOT 
caused by bad subscripting/indexing...)? And, if you can answer in the 
affirmative, what percentage of ALL production errors was this?

Pete.
```
