```
Actually it is _NOT_ irrelevant. The presence of the labels means that
the code must be examined to see if there is any control flow that
will use these.

You may well just glance at the code and say: "I remember not using
that label, therefore it is irrelevant". The next programmer has no
such memory or your not doing that. He has to create his own memory of
it not being used by examining every line of code.

>

…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> paragraphs...

The question was 'falls into'. The option was 'go to'. For every label
that you use the next programmer will have to dertermine whether this
is 'dropped into' or 'go toed'. If the label did not exist as a label
there would be no need to determine this.

> is the "unit" of control transference that we need to be concerned with.
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> such an argument serve?

The choice is 'fall into' or 'go to'. This choice only happens at
labels and is important to resolve in order to determine the flow of
the program.

The fewer labels the less problem in determining how the flow goes.


> Like I said before, this whole discussion would be important if control flow
> was problematic, but for the most part, it isn't. Checks and balances
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> or dropping inot places where it shouldn't.

The problem isn't at tightly controlled sites where there is peer
review. The problem is in code that you may be given with an unknown
history.

Actually the problem _is_ at tightly controlled sites because of the
_cost_ of developing Cobol. There is no automated checks, peer review
doubles development time. Sites switch to more productive languages.

What I am saying is that as long as code is written in archaic and un
productive styles (such as yours) the more likely that Cobol will be
discarded and replaced, as you have done, with Java, C# or others.
```

```
Thanks for the clarification, Bill.  At this point I will publicly state that
my understanding is exactly the same as yours.  And that the coding style I
was taught would not allow for "Para-2" and "Para-3" would have had to be a
"Para-1-EXIT".  Additional Paragraphs would need to be explicitly called
by themselves and include -EXIT Paragraphs.  There was a reason why it was
called Top-down Structured COBOL.

bill
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Bad habits of GOTO-using programmers

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2008-11-10T07:39:26-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1ohgh4tt8l47ojq1vchpfchk0ptus8q0p9@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<d2GPk.7270$cx5.2876@newsfe01.iad> <vvv0h45okn0ta3afojtnh66l6cd9crh1un@4ax.com> <fj12h4pe2rhi2veb7ibgop6i4l319vdd8a@4ax.com> <6ndi1eFkoho1U1@mid.individual.net> <5mg3h4h2jp2irqhf449n9531c0ls00pf6s@4ax.com> <5lk3h4d55e67bsvkftdtfqps3dmvip3tgj@4ax.com> <6ne34vFl6rj3U1@mid.individual.net> <7d19d384-fefa-44dc-ab59-b0319919f08b@s9g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <e2OQk.69329$Fs5.13368@fe10.news.easynews.com>`

```
To me, there are three ways to get into a paragraph.   Either you get
sent there by name (perform, go to), you fall through after performing
the preceding sentence, or you return from a perform or call.

Exit paragraphs don't have performs or calls, so they only have 2
options.
```

```
On Nov 11, 5:57 am, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
> In article <7d19d384-fefa-44dc-ab59-b0319919f...@s9g2000prg.googlegroups.com>,
>         Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> writes:
…[70 more quoted lines elided]…
> duck syndrome" anyway.  :-)

It has nothing to do with how you write your programs. It only has to
do with how Robert's question was answered. He asked how, when there
was a PERFORM xyz THRU xyz-exit, did you get to 'xyz-exit' was it by
GOTO or by dropping into it.

You said 'neither'.

Those two are the only options. Either you have an explicit GOTO _OR_
you run off the end of the paragraph above and 'drop-into' the exit
paragraph (or both may be available).

On another tack I have seen reference to 'paragraphs' where this
included an 'exit label'. Now the term 'Paragraph' is clearly defined
in Cobol and this has one label and a contiguous set of sentences. A
second label, such as 'xyz-exit' starts another distinct paragraph.
```

```
Now. I work according to my client standards most of the time. 
If the client uses sections, then GO are allowed within the Section
mainly to hop to exit paragraph

If he does not use sections, then I only use PERFORM's, and very very
rarely GO TO's.

When doing my OWN programs, I use PERFORM only
```

```
> 1a - do you ever use ALTERs?
-- No
> 1b - if so, do you ever ALTER a PERFORMed label?
-- No
> 1c - do you ever fall through a label that can be ALTERed?
-- No
> 1d - how often do you come across ALTERs in programs you have to maintain?
-- Never
>
> 2a - do you ever GOTO and PERFORM the same label?
-- No
> 2b - do you ever fall through a PERFORMed label?
-- No
>
> 3a - do you use both SECTIONS and paragraph labels in the same program?
> (Exception: SORT INPUT/OUTPUT procedures don't need to be mentioned).
-- Only when working on Telon programs.  Telon gives you a program
structure written in Sections.  One of those Sections is designed to
hold user-written code, which is coded in Paragraphs. At the bottom of
the Telon-generated Section is a Telon-generated paragraph that Abends
if you fall into it.

When not working with Telon, I avoid Sections like the plague.

> 3b - do you always use a terminating label (i.e., 999-X. EXIT.) to indicate
> the end of a PERFORMed paragraph, or a SECTION?  Or sometimes, or never?
-- Never in my own code.  Sometimes when using Telon.
> 3c - do you always, or sometimes, or never, use PERFORM ...THRU i.e., as
> opposed to just PERFORM?
-- Never.  It used to be a shop standard when I started out, but I
have unlearned that behaviour.

> 4a - do you ever use GOTO DEPENDING ON?
-- No.  I used to wish for a PERFORM DEPENDING ON, but that wish has
been answered by EVALUATE.
> 4b - if so, do you ever ALTER any of the target labels?
-- No.
> 4c - are any of the target labels also PERFORMed?
-- No.
> 4d - are any of the target labels also the destination of an unconditional
> GOTO?
-- No.
>
```

```
Paraphrased - Bill Veghte, (an 18-year MS veteran), hesitated when CEO 
Ballmer asked him to oversee sales and marketing for Windows. He 
questioned Ballmer's commitment to the program. He got confirmation and 
authority to make changes in the next version, Windows 7. MS stock has 
fallen more than 30% this year. Windows is 25% of MS revenue. 
Last-quarter sales of Windows for PCs fell short of forecasts after 
sellers of low-cost laptops opted to use the older and cheaper XP 
version, because Vista doesn't run well on their machines.

..... additionally Ballmer selected Steven Sinofsky and John DeVaan, 
engineering chiefs  with track records for streamlining organizations 
and releasing software on time. Windows 7 adds features to more easily 
connect PCs to cameras, printers and home networks. A new design (AGAIN 
!) gives users more options for managing files, folders and windows. 
Windows 7 will start up faster and switch to standby more quickly. The 
software will come out in about a year, which may be earlier than the 
2010 date announced by MS.

Problems getting existing software to work was one of the main reasons 
companies rejected Vista. Only some 26% of business PCs will use Vista 
four years after the release. (Survey by Sanford C. Bernstein & Co). 
Similar study last year predicted 68%.

Citigroup Inc. information chief Jagdish Rao stood beside Ballmer in NY 
in Jan 2007 pledging to put Vista on the bank's more than 350,000 
computers. Two years later, less than 10% of Citigroup's machines run 
Vista as employees struggle to make it work with Citigroup's more than 
11,000 applications.

And it goes on and on.......

*********************

I don't care if Paul is running N/E 3.1 or N/E 5.1 - he would be well 
advised to give Vista a miss !

Jimmy
```

```
See for example:

	http://marketshare.hitslink.com/report.aspx?qprid=8

While I'm quite suspicious of these statistics, I suspect they're
correct within an order of magnitude; I doubt Linux penetration in the
 desktop market is more than a few percentage points at best. However,
I know tens, perhaps a hundred, Linux desktop users, while I can't
think of anyone still running OS/2 (or eComStation).

I suspect a much stronger driver for allowing XP on new machines was
that Vista's unpopularity was having a (perceived) chilling effect on
the market for upgrading to new hardware - ie, people would stick with
their old machines running XP rather than buy flashy new hardware if
they had to move to Vista in the process.
```

```
Paul H wrote:
> "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net> wrote in message
> news:49112005$0$20972$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net...
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> versions Vista issues.

It's not a Vista flaw - it's a flaw in the installer. The installer checks 
for IE version and doesn't recognize the designation used by Microsoft for 
IE7. From the Microsoft Developer Network:

"Several typical browser detection techniques fail when they encounter 
Internet Explorer 7. For example, some sites that look great when viewed in 
Microsoft Internet Explorer 6 might display errors when viewed in Internet 
Explorer 7. This is not caused by any problems with Internet Explorer 7; it 
happens because the browser detection code is not designed for newer 
versions of the browser."


There might be a hack/circumvention for defective installers.
```

<a id="t-908468bb04"></a>
## Thread: [OT] Geezer Error-Message — 2 msgs

### [OT] Geezer Error-Message

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2008-11-06T14:04:55+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<geuti7$q1q$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```

My current client manages source code with Librarian; a program starts out 
in NODE1.NODE2.DEV.SOURCE(MEMBER), compiled to 
NODE1.NODE2.DEV.LOAD(MEMBER) gets a user sign-off, gets copied from there 
to NODE2.NODE3.XFER.SOURCE(MEMBER) and then Ops runs a job to copy it to 
NODE1.NODE2.PRD.SOURCE(MEMBER) and another job to compile it to 
NODE1.NODE2.PRD.LOAD(MEMBER)... and from there it is run as a Prod job.

Something needed to be changed recently and I notified Ops that it was 
ready to roll... and just for laughs checked the SYSOUT to see what was 
happening.

(I was given browse-authorisation to the Ops output jobs because Ops 
realised it would be a Very Good Thing if I were allowed to look at Prod 
runs so I could see what blew up from my own seat rather than having one 
of them wander over and log on or having me wander over to where they 
live.)

The proc they ran to copy the job from XFER to PRD executed a program 
named ESCLIBR... and the messages a true glimpse Into The Past.  Never 
mind that there was 'THE LIBRARIAN SOURCE MANAGEMENT SYSTEM IS LEASED FROM 
APPLIED DATA RESEARCH, INC.' and the Customer Support Information included 
a Telex number - browsing the load module revealed a 'short rec' ending 
with X'84110F' (19 Apr 1984) - but the last message in the job gave me 
cause to chuckle:

'WARNING: FINAL INPUT STATEMENT WAS NOT -END.  MAKE SURE THAT LAST PART OF 
DECK WAS NOT DROPPED.'

DD
```

<a id="t-5b01196884"></a>
## Thread: z/OS static linking — 11 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### z/OS static linking

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-10T18:28:56-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49187D68.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
With VSE if you want to include a separately compiled 'static' module with
your program the static module must compiled into an object module and then
cataloged to a library.

This also works with z/OS.  Yet it appears that you can also link a "fully
linked" load module and it appears to function just as well.

Please don't focus too much on my very like erroneous terminology, but feel
free to correct it.

Let me give an example...  We have a sub-routine called SUBR (yes, it
actually is called SUBR!).  We can do a compile followed by a link and place
the resulting module in to a load library.

A separate program can do a dynamic call of SUBR (either by using CALL
literal or by using the DYNAM compile option).

What surprised me is I can also do a static call of the routine, and during
the link-edit (bind) step, as long as the library that contains the SUBR
load module is in my SYSLIN concatenation the SUBR routine is automatically
linked in to my final load module.  As far as I know this is not possible
under VSE.  You can only link object modules together, not load modules
('phases' in VSE).

Is this something that has always been available for MVS and its successors,
or is this something fairly "new" (where new might mean within the last 20
years!).

I just want to make sure this isn't "too good to be true".  All my testing
indicates that it works, but I don't know if there are cases where it
won't.

Don't get me wrong, I am happy it works the way it does.  If we ever wanted
to do something like this on VSE we had to have both the OBJ and the PHASE
versions of the modules available.  This way we need only one.

Oh, and don't take this as saying we do a lot of static calls.  We prefer
dynamic generally, especially for those modules that actually change.  But
the SUBR module hasn't changed in ten years, other than to add a few new
subroutines.  Now we can leave them as static calls and still have the
ability to change them later to dynamic calls, while requiring only having
the single compiled module.

I should note that SUBR is written in assembler.  I haven't tried it yet
with a Cobol subroutine.  I'm assuming it will work as well, but who knows.

Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: z/OS static linking

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-11T05:03:19+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<qm8Sk.168100$Xn4.149135@fe09.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<49187D68.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Frank,
  Yes, under "MVS" (aka z/OS) you can "link-edit" (or more recently "bind") 
either object modules or load modules.  If you are using a mixture of object 
modules and load modules, you need to be "careful' about which version of 
identically named object code you bring into the final module. 
(Link-edit/binder statements control this).

I couldn't tell from your statement if you were trying to do "dynamic" calls to 
a program that was STATICALLY link-edited (bound) into the same load module. 
This is a VERY dangerous thing to do and can lead to "unpredictable" results. 
Similar unpredictable results happen if you "mix" static and dynamic calls to 
the same subprogram in an application, i.e

Compile with NODYNAM and
  call "literal-1"
    and
   call identifier-1  *>  with "literal-1" as the value of identifier-1

There used to be some warnings about this somewhere in the COBOL programming 
guide.  If you need me to, I can try and find it.

P.S.  In z/OS shops, it is actually VERY unusual to "archive" object modules; 
usually, only load modules are maintained/retained - because there is not need 
to retain object modules for "future use".  Some shops keep object modules, but 
certainly not most that I know of.
```

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> With VSE if you want to include a separately compiled 'static' module with
> your program the static module must compiled into an object module and then
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> linked" load module and it appears to function just as well.

I've only worked in one MVS-z/OS shop, and anytime a subprogram is 
compiled (assembler or COBOL) it is link-edited into a Load Library.  As 
Bill Klein points out, it is certainly possible to store object modules 
in libraries under z/OS, and to link from them, but it doesn't appear to 
be the standard practice.

> 
> Please don't focus too much on my very like erroneous terminology, but feel
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> years!).

I've only worked in one MVS-z/OS shop, and anytime a subprogram is 
compiled (assembler or COBOL) it is link-edited into a Load Library.  As 
Bill Klein points out, it is certainly possible to store object modules 
in libraries under z/OS, and to link from them, but it doesn't appear to 
be the standard practice, at least in my limited experience.

> 
> I just want to make sure this isn't "too good to be true".  All my testing
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Yes, it does work exactly the same way for COBOL.  You can dynamically 
call a COBOL subprogram from a load library, or you can statically link 
the same COBOL subprogram into a main program.

There's another useful trick you can do with the IBM linkage editor 
(both the old version or the new Binder, as far as I know).  If you have 
an old main program with a statically bound subprogram, and a new 
version of the subprogram, you can run the Linkage Editor (IEWL?) with 
commands to tell it to REMOVE the statically bound subprogram from the 
main program and REPLACE it with the new version of that subprogram. 
You don't need to recompile the main program in order to link in the new 
subprogram.

You do need to be careful, I remember a case where we had a main program 
that called subprograms B, C, and D (all static calls), but D also 
called B.  A careless programmer managed to get a different version of B 
linked to D, from B linked to the main program.  So depending on whether 
B was called by the main program or by the D subprogram, you got 
different results.

That was 20 years ago.  Now our standard is all dynamic calls and the 
problem does not arise.

Regards,
```

#### ↳ Re: z/OS static linking

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-11-11T21:52:47-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ftdkh4lp50i714d42cmrqspb0dgot7o61n@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<49187D68.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
On Mon, 10 Nov 2008 18:28:56 -0700, "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
wrote:

>With VSE if you want to include a separately compiled 'static' module with
>your program the static module must compiled into an object module and then
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>('phases' in VSE).

Unix does not allow static linking of code packaged for dynamic loading .. on purpose. 
If it did, people would abuse deployment by statically linking third party libraries. I've
seen quite a few failed attempts. One well-known global company, which thinks static
linking is a religion, routinely deploys executables sized at 150-200 meg. About  half is
dead code that can never be executed, linked because they include whole libraries to get
one function. Unix comes with a 'strip' tool that removes dead code. It shrinks their
executables to 75-100 meg. They refuse to use it; I guess it's against their religion. 

It used to be semi-common practice to statically link the Micro Focus runtime, which adds
1 meg. You can't do that any longer. 

Defenders of static linking usually give excuses 1. or 2. below; they refuse to admit the
real reason, 3. below.

1. Faster calls. 
    At program start time, Unix loads ALL dynamic libraries and puts entry point addresses
into the symbol table, in the same place where static goes. Calls are exactly the same
speed. The only way they might be slower is when the function name is a variable that must
be looked up. Cobol is the only language (I know of) that encourages, but does not
require, dynamic calls through a variable (which, in fact, doesn't change). The solution
is to use linker options to make Cobol calls dynamic, not by calling through a variable. 

2. Security
  The're afraid a bad guy will put a function containing malware ahead of the real one in
the execution time search path. The Unix linker has many ways of preventing this from
happening, including remembering the directory where the function was at link time,
remembering the version number and remembering the function's hash. Windows has even
better safeguards available (although they're seldom used). 

3. Poor control over deployment and execution environment
  They've been frustrated by jobs failing because the wrong library was loaded at
execution time. Rather than deal with the actual problem, they statically link everything
they can, including functions in sister systems called by the executable, which in turn
call second cousin systems. Companies that do this are also unable to keep dependencies
straight, so wind up doing weekly 'full builds' taking 15 hours.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: z/OS static linking

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-12T11:06:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gffe891uud@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<ftdkh4lp50i714d42cmrqspb0dgot7o61n@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<49187D68.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <ftdkh4lp50i714d42cmrqspb0dgot7o61n@4ax.com>`

```
Robert wrote:
> 
> Unix does not allow static linking of code packaged for dynamic loading

Some Unix variants do. The Unix specification (SUSv3) doesn't cover
linking, except for specifying the interface for the dlfcn API, the
actual behavior of which is left to the implementation.

On any Unix platform, there's nothing to prevent creating additional
executable formats, which could support both static and dynamic
linking of a single module.

> Unix comes with a 'strip' tool that removes dead code.

strip removes unneeded symbol entries. I'm not aware of any
implementation that removes dead code.

SUSv3 leaves it up to the implementation what strip removes, but says
that its "effect ... shall be similar to the -s option to c99". In
historical and mainstream contemporary compilers, -s removes unneeded
symbol information, such as debug symbols.

The GNU strip only removes symbols.

> At program start time, Unix loads ALL dynamic libraries

Except for the ones that it doesn't. Various Unix dynamic loaders
support various kinds of deferred loading. And pretty much all of them
support explicit loading as well, which obviously doesn't happen at
program start time.

> and puts entry point addresses into the symbol table, in the same
> place where static goes. Calls are exactly the same
> speed.

This is not true for ELF, which is probably the dominant Unix
dynamic-linked binary format. See for example the discussion in:

	http://vx.netlux.org/lib/vsc06.html

In ELF, each shared object used by one or more active processes will
have a shared Procedure Linkage Table, and each process has a private
Global Offset Table.

By default, the first call to an entry point in a shared object will
invoke the runtime dynamic linker to resolve the symbol and put its
address in the GOT, using information from the PLT. The PLT can't be
changed to point directly to the entry point, because it's shared by
other processes, which may have loaded the shared object at different
addresses.

On subsequent calls, the entry point can be called through the address
in the GOT. Note, however, that this is still an indirect call, unlike
a call to a statically-linked entry point.

There's an environmental option to force early binding of GOT entries,
but it's rarely used. Lazy binding is the default.

Most other Unix dynamic linking implementations are similar. AIX's
XCOFF model, for example, uses indirect calls through the TOC (Table
Of Contents) section.
```

```
could.
```

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<k82nh4p7o8bc4j8hg9fvu1lnrg0rd89s23@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<49187D68.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <ftdkh4lp50i714d42cmrqspb0dgot7o61n@4ax.com> <491B0049.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
On Wed, 12 Nov 2008 16:11:53 -0700, "Frank Swarbrick"
<Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote:

>>>> On 11/11/2008 at 7:52 PM, in message
><ftdkh4lp50i714d42cmrqspb0dgot7o61n@4ax.com>, Robert<no@e.mail> wrote:
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
>dynamic linking.  Other than that I don't see much reason for it.

In testing the performance of a date routine that I wrote for the Year
2000 project I was on, I found that there was a repeatable and
measurable difference between a dynamic call and a call to a nested
program.  The date routine was set up in a copybook and a program with
the same name copied it as the only program.  This way to get the
performance increase, all that had to be done was copy the date
routine in the last statement of the program.  Much to my surprise
there was both an initial overhead and a per call overhead to the
dynamic link.  Note that the overhead was not enough in any shop that
I have been in to cause the batch compile default to be NODYNAM.  CICS
compiles must be NODYNAM unless the rules have changed.
>
>Frank
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: I need to see changes to source code...

```
On Thu, 13 Nov 2008 09:20:15 +0100, Alain Reymond <arwebmail@skynet.be> wrote:

>Paul H a ï¿½crit :
>> I could use a utility that compares two Cobol source programs, showing 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>http://winmerge.org/

Judging from the screen shots http://winmerge.org/about/screenshots/, winmerge looks a lot
like Beyond Compare. Key features:

.. Manually (or automatically?) MERGE differences. 

.. Compare entire DIRECTORY trees, not just two files. 
    So you can answer the question 'What's the difference between 3.0 and 3.1?', 
    referring to a large system, not one program.

Compare tools look especially polished when linked to GUI version control, such as WinCVS.
You start with a graphic treeview of a program's versions, select two of them, perhaps in
different branches, hit one key and SHAZAM, see the differences on one screen, in context
and color coded. 

Typically, WinCVS and the compare tool run on your Windows desktop, the repository is on a
remote Unix server, the programs' target machine can be anything, even a mainframe.
```

## Thread: creating hash code of a txt file — 9 this month / 10 total

### creating hash code of a txt file

- **From:** mario <mmc_vw1200@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-12T06:58:15-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
hi

i´d like to create a hash-code of a txt-file.
is there a standard function in cobol that can handle that?

thanks a LOT

mario
```

#### ↳ Re: creating hash code of a txt file

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-12T09:35:55-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<uaGdnW1C_LBHaofUnZ2dnUVZ_oDinZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
mario wrote:
> hi
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

No, but it's relatively easy.

01  HASH-NUMBER-X.
   02  HASH-NUMBER  PIC 9.
01  HASH-SUM   PIC 9(6).


MOVE FILE-RECORD-X(I) TO HASH-NUMBER-X.
ADD HASH-NUMBER TO HASH-SUM.

Some compilers or run-times may fuss about the above construct for any of 
several reasons, but it should be a simple matter to fool the system.

If you're concerned with security, another way is to store the date the file 
was modified as part of the file itself. The date modified is part of the 
file's directory entry and is moderately difficult to hack. When you access 
the file, you can compare it's stored modification date with the one 
recorded by the system.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: creating hash code of a txt file

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-11-12T17:39:08-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0lmmh4h2lft9dmqc8b6untecnl4fplmlmn@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <uaGdnW1C_LBHaofUnZ2dnUVZ_oDinZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On Wed, 12 Nov 2008 09:35:55 -0600, "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:

>If you're concerned with security, another way is to store the date the file 
>was modified as part of the file itself. The date modified is part of the 
>file's directory entry and is moderately difficult to hack. When you access 
>the file, you can compare it's stored modification date with the one 
>recorded by the system.

It takes one line of code in Pascal:

FileSetDate (vHandle, DateTimeToFileDate ((StrToDateTime (vTimestamp))));

How hard was that?
```

#### ↳ Re: creating hash code of a txt file

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-14T22:15:09-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gfnns801dd8@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
mario wrote:
> 
> iï¿½d like to create a hash-code of a txt-file.
> is there a standard function in cobol that can handle that?

First, of course, you can't have a function in standard COBOL that
does anything to a file, because files aren't first-class objects in
COBOL. A COBOL function (or procedure) could operate on the *data*
from a file.

Second, "hash-code" means almost nothing without further
qualification. A hash is just the result of applying some function to
the input; usually it has the property that the range is smaller than
the domain, so multiple inputs give the same output (and so the
function is a one-way, aka lossy, compression function), but that's
not strictly required.

Adding the values of the bytes in the file, as HeyBub suggested, is a
valid hash function. Whether it meets your requirements we can't say,
since you didn't tell us anything about them.

You also didn't tell us what platform you're using. Many have a
variety of hash functions that can be called from COBOL, either as
part of the operating system or freely available.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: creating hash code of a txt file

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-18T07:36:16-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<lrydnaO-YsNMWb_UnZ2dnUVZ_tzinZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <gfnns801dd8@news3.newsguy.com> <22efa98e-3247-4e6d-8d01-3b6216a5db93@a17g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`

```
mario wrote:
> Hi tks for the help...
>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>

So incorporate the notion of a check-digit to catch transposition problems. 
When creating the sum, multiply each character by the successive digits 
0,1,2,...9,0,1,2...

Let A=1, B=2, C=3

Then the hash for the file containing "ABC" becomes (1*0 + 2*1 + 3*2) = 8

While the file containing "CBA" becomes (3*0 + 2*1 + 1*2) = 4

If you limit the check-digit to one digit, you decrease the chance of an 
undetected error by 90%. Using two digits, you get 99% reliability. And so 
on.

Or (easier) just leave it up to the customer to catch the problem.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: creating hash code of a txt file

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@worldnet.att.net>
- **Date:** 2008-11-19T05:40:39+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<rFNUk.141308$Mh5.76241@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<22efa98e-3247-4e6d-8d01-3b6216a5db93@a17g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <gfnns801dd8@news3.newsguy.com> <22efa98e-3247-4e6d-8d01-3b6216a5db93@a17g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`

```
mario wrote:
> Hi tks for the help...
> 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> mario

You don't say what environment you need this for.

It sounds like you're looking for a Checksum or CRC algorithm.  You 
might be able to find something on the web for free, or you might want 
to have some fun coding your own routine.

Many years ago I worked in a programming group that used its own 
homegrown checksum/crc algorithm to create 32-bit checksums for each 
source code file.  It was used mostly to verify that the correct version 
of a source code program was promoted from test into production.

Wikipedia has an article on the subject that you might find to be 
informative:
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclic_redundancy_check

With kindest regards,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: creating hash code of a txt file

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-19T11:11:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gg1goe018k9@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<22efa98e-3247-4e6d-8d01-3b6216a5db93@a17g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<0629c5d2-d217-484c-9430-6263d07801d1@n33g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <gfnns801dd8@news3.newsguy.com> <22efa98e-3247-4e6d-8d01-3b6216a5db93@a17g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`

```
mario wrote:
> 
> I need a unique hash value of a file, so that i can control if a file
> is unique,

It's impossible to have a unique hash value for any file, unless the
hash output is at least as long as the file, by the pigeonhole principle.

If you have other guarantees about the nature of the input files, you
can create a unique hash value for each file (a "perfect hash"), but
usually the best you can do is use a hash that has a very low
probability of duplicate values for different files ("collisions").

> the problem in the methode of summing the chars is that the file
> considering   "adam loves eva" brings the same hash value as "eva
> loves adam" even if these are to very different files!

Yes, that's a collision. A simple checksum isn't very
collision-resistant, because addition is commutative.

> my program imports files, and i need to ensure that one file will not
> be importet twice,  for example 2008_09_data,  2008_10_data, but it
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> pretty save but not to 100%

You probably can't have 100%. You can reduce the probability of a
collision, though. An MD5 hash, for example, has an extremely low
probability of accidental collision, and even creating a deliberate
collision (a "chosen-preimage collision") is difficult.[1]

There are probably MD5 implementations available for your platform,
which you can call from your COBOL program. But you still haven't told
us what your platform is, or what COBOL implementation you're using,
so we can only guess.

It's possible, though non-trivial, to implement MD5 yourself in at
least some versions of COBOL. I wrote an implementation for MF COBOL
that's freely available; I posted it to the MF Forums.[2] If you're
not using MF COBOL, though, porting it to another COBOL dialect is
probably more trouble than it's worth.

There are a great many hash algorithms between simple modular
checksums and cryptographic hashes like MD5. Arnold Trembley mentioned
CRCs, which are more resistant to collisions than checksums. A good
CRC-32 (eg, the IEEE polynomial) should have close to probability
N*2**-16 of an accidental collision, where N is the number of files;
so if you test 256 files, for example, you should have about a 99.6%
chance that they'll have different hash values. Even with 2048 files,
the chance is about 96.9% that they'll have different hashes.

Calculating a CRC in COBOL won't be much fun, but it's doable.

Adler-32 is less collision-resistant than a good CRC-32, but it's
easier and faster to calculate. You should be able to implement it
pretty easily in COBOL, especially since Part A of the Adler checksum
is the sum (mod 2**16) of the bytes in the data, which is similar to
your existing code.

See eg http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adler-32.

Note that Adler-32 would be a poor choice if the files are very short.
It works best if the typical length of the files is at least about
5600 bytes.


[1] Not as difficult as it should be, which is why MD5 is no longer
recommended for cryptographic applications, but hard enough for most
purposes.

[2] Go to http://forum.microfocus.com:8088/~Micro_Focus_Products/login
and log in, or create an account if you don't have one. Go to the
"Micro Focus Products" board and search for MD5.
```

## Thread: Sample for testing "backwards" perform thru — 15 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### Sample for testing "backwards" perform thru

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-13T22:10:55+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<PB1Tk.165855$f64.161933@fe05.news.easynews.com>`

```
There were some comments about testing support for and behavior of
  Perform procedure-name-1 thru procedure-name-2
where Procedure-name-2 actually appears BEFORE procedure-name-1 in the source 
code.

The following is a sample program to test whether "your" compiler supports this 
(compiles it) and what happens.  I *think* (but won't swear to it) that all 
(most?) compilers will compile this and display "In para4" and then terminate.
 (I don't think that such compiler options as "perform-type" (MF) or OPT (IBM) 
will impact this, but I won't swear to that).

  * * *

       Identification Division.
        Program-ID. PERFTHRU.
       Procedure Division.
        Mainline.
           Display "In Mainline"
           Perform Para3 Thru Para1
           Display "After Perform"
           Stop Run
            .
       Para1.
           Display "In para1"
             .
       Para2.
           Display "In Para2"
              .
       Para3.
           Display "In para3"
             .
       Para4.
           Display "In para4"
             .
       Alternate-End.
           Stop Run
             .
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Sample for testing "backwards" perform thru

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-14T10:24:03-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b6a83b60-3b56-4cb6-afbb-2dacb622c609@s1g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<PB1Tk.165855$f64.161933@fe05.news.easynews.com> <6o5g5jF1ul34U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Nov 15, 3:26 am, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
> In article <PB1Tk.165855$f64.161...@fe05.news.easynews.com>,
>         "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.netcom.com> writes:
…[59 more quoted lines elided]…
> But then, isn't that what "Undefined" means?  :-)

TinyCobol is very broken then. It has made an arbitrary decision to
not execute the code in Para4. For example it may have been that in
Para4 there was a GO TO Para1 that completed the PERFORM.

What _is_ clearly defined is that with a PERFORM THRU the path should
'drop through' until the end point is reached (granted it never could
be in this test).

Cobol also states what happens when a STOP RUN is reached (in
Alternate end) or if the path falls off the end of the code (ie if the
Alternate-end paragraph was removed).

The other compilers get it right.


> I can't make any promises but it would be fun to try it with things like
> some of my old Ryan-McFarland compilers and maybe the one from OpenVMS
> or one of the old PDP-11 COBOL's. Could be interesting to see just how
> many different ways there are to nterpret the concept.

There is only one way to correctly interpret what happens with the
code. There is nothing to direct the path of execution back to Para1
before the STOP RUN or 'falling off the end'. Neither of which will
cause an error message.

Would you hope to find a compiler that did it differently ?

Do you think that TinyCobol is better ?

I certainly don't want arbitrary decisions made that make programs do
something different from what the source code indicates will happen.
```

```
grants rather a wide latitude for interpretation.

>                                  It has made an arbitrary decision to
> not execute the code in Para4. For example it may have been that in
> Para4 there was a GO TO Para1 that completed the PERFORM.

Ah!!  Now I see what you mean.  Interesting point and, I would have to agree.
They probably don't care, but I will likely forward this on to them.

> What _is_ clearly defined is that with a PERFORM THRU the path should
> 'drop through' until the end point is reached (granted it never could
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> The other compilers get it right.

Yeah, in light of the standard.  But I think the compiler should have done
like the Fuji-Siemens and at least told the programmer his code was crap
and give him a chance to fix it.  :-)  I am also glad (even though I really
don't get to do COBOL for a living anymore) that I never worked ina shop
that would have allowed something like this anywhere near a compiler.

>> I can't make any promises but it would be fun to try it with things like
>> some of my old Ryan-McFarland compilers and maybe the one from OpenVMS
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Would you hope to find a compiler that did it differently ?

As I said, I would hope to never see code like this and I think, given the
availability of resources today, that all compilers should point out the
folly of this kind of code.

> Do you think that TinyCobol is better ?

Didn't say anything was better or worse.  I just ran it thru the two
compilers I have close at hand and posted the results.  When I have
time I will run it thru other (usually much older) compilers just to
see what happens.

> I certainly don't want arbitrary decisions made that make programs do
> something different from what the source code indicates will happen.

And, of course, the real solution to this is don't write arbitrary code.
Write you programs to do what you want and only what you want and you
will never have to worry about the compiler making arbitrary decisions.

bill
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Sample for testing "backwards" perform thru

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-14T10:36:46-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e15e0a39-666d-453f-99b8-74f0e783b154@o4g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<PB1Tk.165855$f64.161933@fe05.news.easynews.com> <em7rh4drc8in7l5c88osb218tbm0n8lrrj@4ax.com>`

```
On Nov 15, 4:54 am, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Thu, 13 Nov 2008 22:10:55 GMT, "William M. Klein"
>
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> An alternate to test would be to have Para3 go to or perform Para 1.

A goto Para1 in Para3 or Para4, or at any chosen point afterwards (but
before the stop run), will complete the PERFORM correctly. However, it
may be that the broken TinyCobol may not get to the goto before
deciding that is what will be done.

'Perform Para1' will not, or should not, complete the original
perform. Overlapping performs have an undefined result. In most modern
compilers the perform stack will ensure that only the current return
is seen and the previous one is restored. You should see 'Para1'
before the program continues as before to 'alternate-end'.

For a few ancient compilers that use self-modifying code, such as XE13
on 1900 series, the return point in Para1 will be overwritten by the
new perform and a subsequent 'go to Para1' will find that there is no
longer a return at the end of Para1 and that the original perform will
not complete, the path dropping through all again and finding the 'go
to Para1' each time.

> (at least for the compilers that do not return to display "After
> perform").



> --
> "In no part of the constitution is more wisdom to be found,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> - James Madison
```

```
For compilers of this era and maybe lots of others, probably the "correct"
return code would be generated at the end of para1, but since the program
flow never gets there it never gets executed.

of order is needed then seperate performs would be used, almost intuitively,
I'd think.
```

## Thread: Sample for testing "backwards" .... — 1 msgs

### Re: Sample for testing "backwards" ....

- **From:** "WhatKindOfFoolAmI" <WhatKindOfFool@AmI.net>
- **Date:** 2008-11-14T02:56:49-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f264f$491d3d55$62148c58$7375@ALLTEL.NET>`

```
------------------

  * * *

       Identification Division.
        Program-ID. PERFTHRU.
       Procedure Division.
        Mainline.
           Display "In Mainline"
           Perform Para3 Thru Para1
           Display "After Perform"
           Stop Run
            .
       Para1.
           Display "In para1"
             .
       Para2.
           Display "In Para2"
              .
       Para3.
           Display "In para3"
             .
       Para4.
           Display "In para4"
             .
       Alternate-End.
           Stop Run
             .
```

### OpenCOBOL Compile Problem

```
before,
but when I go to do it now, I get the following oddball error. This
happens with 1.1 and 1.0.

Help?

fileio.c:310: error: expected specifier-qualifier-list before 'DB'

I have looked at the code, and I just don't see anything there that
should be causing this, but then, I am not exactly sure what the error
*is* either. It will generate a few hundred lines of errors later in
the same module.

Thanks
-Paul
```

#### ↳ Re: OpenCOBOL Compile Problem

- **From:** btiffin@canada.com
- **Date:** 2008-11-14T14:10:11-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a87a2c43-f59a-485b-bc92-36333d9fa492@b31g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<f226ab55-2de0-4a71-8610-513e9b0677b8@r36g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Nov 14, 12:50 pm, PR <paul.rauler...@gmail.com> wrote:
> I *know* I have compiled OpenCOBOL on Itanium Linux (Suse 10.2)
> before,
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> -Paul

This looks like a Berkley DataBase versioning issue.  BDB is fairly
fragile when in comes to compatibility between versions.

In fileio.c take a look at the lines from 60 ish to 90 ish.  You'll
see a fairly complex set of conditional includes for the variants of
db.h

From ./configure --help

  --with-cisam            (OpenCOBOL) Use CISAM for ISAM I/O
  --with-disam            (OpenCOBOL) Use DISAM for ISAM I/O
  --with-vbisam           (OpenCOBOL) Use VBISAM for ISAM I/O
  --with-index-extfh      (OpenCOBOL) Use external ISAM file handler
  --with-db1              (OpenCOBOL) use Berkeley DB 1.85
(libdb-1.85)
  --with-db               (OpenCOBOL) use Berkeley DB 3.0 or later
(libdb)(default)
  --with-lfs64            (OpenCOBOL) use large file system for file I/
O (default)

So you may want to try --with-db1  or  --with-db  depending on your
local Berkeley setup.  Or install a newer libdb.  I've never
encountered problems, but this one comes up quite often.

Cheers,
Brian
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: OpenCOBOL Compile Problem

- **From:** "Roger While" <simrw@sim-basis.de>

```
4.x BDB installed.

eg. From rpm package list -

db-devel-4.2.52-85

All quite clearly stated in the README (1.1).

Roger
```

## Thread: NEWS for Fujtissu COBOL users — 13 this month / 27 total

### NEWS for Fujtissu COBOL users

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-14T21:57:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xvmTk.163083$Fs5.45452@fe10.news.easynews.com>`

```
I don't know how "current" you need to be to receive Fujitsu emails, but if you 
haven't hears, please read the press release at:

     http://www.netcobol.com/news/prelease/pr20081113.htm

To me PERSONALLY, the thing that will be MOST interesting is that "NetCOBOL" 
(outside of Japan) will no longer be able to rely on other Fujitsu 
software/hardware to support its development and maintenance.  With no "real" 
evidence, I have always viewed Fujitsu's Windows and Unix/Linux COBOL as a "lost 
leader" (if that's the term) and seriously doubted that it had a business case 
that could break even, much less make a profit.  To me, this was part of (most 
of?) the reason that Micro Focus had run-time fees and Fujitsu did NOT.

It will be very interesting to me to see if "Alchemy Solutions"
 - keeps run-time support FREE
    and
 - breaks even / makes a profit

They certainly must have a business plan that seems reasonable to one "investor 
group" but I am certainly curious how this will play out over the short, medium, 
and long-term
```

```
My experience has been that there has been very little "development and 
maintenance" of the Fujitsu product in the USA. I don't see the formation of 
Alchemy as changing this very much. It is very likely that ties between 
Alcemy and Fujitsu Japan will remain good, even if the new company is 
officially separate.



> With no "real" evidence, I have always viewed Fujitsu's Windows and 
> Unix/Linux COBOL as a "lost leader" (if that's the term) and seriously 
> doubted that it had a business case that could break even, much less make 
> a profit.

The term is "loss leader"... a product offered at/below cost to entice 
people into buying other products.

I don't think that is a fair description of the Fujitsu Products, Bill.

These are excellent products. There is no real reason why Alchemy should not 
be able to sell them and make a profit.

The problem in the past has been very poor support, and a disastrous cost 
cutting exercise that saw many of the people who really were able to support 
the products laid off.

I see two things as leading to the erosion of the Fujitsu customer base:

1. Absolutely abysmal support.
2. The introduction of the remote licensing system, which treated the user 
base as if they were criminals and put mission critical applications at risk 
for anyone foolish enough to use Fujitsu COBOL as the basis for their 
business. Despite the "30 day" assurance, no-one running a business is going 
to be happy with not being able to have COBOL installed on backup servers 
that can be switched in immediately should a problem arise. The process 
assumed that the regular customer base was pirating the software; a false 
assumption in my experience. I know and have worked with a number of Fujitsu 
customers and none of them would consider distributing illegal copies of the 
software.

Despite the fact that this system can be easily circumvented using modern 
approaches, I don't know of anyone (self included :-)) who would do that in 
order to on-sell the product.

Alchemy is going to focus on Migration of COBOL apps and they have some very 
good tools for doing this. They are still MicroSoft partners and that should 
be of considerable benefit in the marketplace they are now focusing on.

It isn't just about selling COBOL compilers.

> To me, this was part of (most of?) the reason that Micro Focus had 
> run-time fees and Fujitsu did NOT.
>
I disagree. Not requiring Run Time fees was a very important competitive 
edge for Fujitsu to get a share of the workstation COBOL market.

Unfortunately, they eroded that share because of the problems mentioned 
above.

Micro Focus have aggressively marketed their product to a business user base 
who will swallow the Run Time fees, as long as they get proper support for 
the products.  Let's face it, the road for Micro Focus has not been an easy 
one either; they have had their share of upheaval and mismanagement. Stephen 
Kelly has done and is doing an excellent job of re-inventing the company and 
he is an outstanding CEO. Ron Langer, who has taken the reins for Alchemy, 
is also a very experienced and competent manager who has probably learned 
much and recognised mistakes from the past with the Fujitsu products. The 
fact that Langer has decided to split Migration into a separate company 
(Alchemy) is a good move which enables renewed focus. With adequate 
financial backing, and proper investment and direction, I see Alchemy as 
having good potential; there is no need for them to license the Run Time, 
any more than there is really a need for Micro Focus to do so.  (Despite 
their protestations to the contrary, RT fees are really just icing for MF. 
The real money is in providing tools, services, and consultancy.)

We cannot know how many prospects decided against MF BECAUSE of the Run Time 
fees. The company opted to focus on a market that will pay these fees as a 
cost of doing business, PROVIDED they get proper support and assistance 
(which MF are providing very well).

In the broad scheme of things, Run Time fees are neither here nor there.

Both companies have realised that COBOL is in decline. Despite talking it 
up, nobody is really fooled into thinking they can continue COBOL 
development indefinitely. There is a VAST market in assisting people (mainly 
Mainframe sites, but not exclusively...) to move away from COBOL. No-one 
wants to throw away their existing investment, and neither should they have 
to, but the end result is simply inevitable. The procedural paradigm is not 
going to serve businesses into this century. Anyone who can provide tools 
and support to ease the transition into Objects, is likely to make a very 
good living :-) (I am currently devoting ALL of my surplus time and energy 
into doing this and the results are "promising". Both MF and Fujitsu have 
excellent established tools for moving COBOL to a networked platform, but 
this is just the beginning. The end point has to be layers and objects 
(whether existing COBOL users can see it or not... :-))

> It will be very interesting to me to see if "Alchemy Solutions"
> - keeps run-time support FREE
>    and
> - breaks even / makes a profit

Well, I wish them well and I really hope they do. It would be very bad for 
what's left of this marketplace if it was dominated entirely by ONE service 
provider.
>
> They certainly must have a business plan that seems reasonable to one 
> "investor group" but I am certainly curious how this will play out over 
> the short, medium, and long-term
>

I wouldn't mind betting that they are too... :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Nov 14, 5:40 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
> In the broad scheme of things, Run Time fees are neither here nor there.
>
They actually still are there.  I know of some vertical market package
vendors who migrated to a PC COBOL product to quickly jump from a very
old platform to a newer Unix/Linux platform.  They even added some GUI
and Web type interfaces to it.  All in all, it turned out pretty
nice.  That said, at least one of the companies is bleeding badly
right now.  They have had to roll the run-time licensing fees into
their annual support contract.  While the initial system is priced on
par with their competitors, when a five-year cost of ownership layed
out, they lose.  The other vendors went with native compilers and
don't have to roll in the additional run-time fees as part of their
support contract.

> Both companies have realised that COBOL is in decline. Despite talking it
> up, nobody is really fooled into thinking they can continue COBOL
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I would like the opportunity to disagree with this one.  Yes, there
are a lot of companies hiring object programmers and yes, I do object
programming, but a lot of those "new tool" projects are failing.  I'm
talking the kind of spectacular failure which used to only surface
once every couple of years now happening repeatedly at the same
companies.

Would I agree that "indexed file" COBOL is going away?  Mostly.  COBOL
is moving from the foreground to the background.  When companies
implement SOA correctly, almost all of the COBOL still keeps running.
Now it just communicates through port services or MQ Series.

There currently are some very disturbing trends in the industry.
1)  Choosing tools for projects based upon what the $10/day labor
market knows
2)  Hiding failed projects in the financial filings
3)  Directly connecting Web applications to databases which have
sensitive (privacy lawsuit) type data

The first two are really related and have lead to some companies being
audited and delisted.  The third one has very few books written about
what a bad idea it is.  There are a great many books written about HOW
to do it, but apparently the authors aren't technically savy enough to
know it should never be done.

just my 0.02
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: NEWS for Fujitsu COBOL users

```
This is a very good response. Thank you.

I've added some comments below.

"yyyc186" <yyyc186@hughes.net> wrote in message 
news:c31bf062-8ce8-476c-8b6c-25b02fb0c456@s9g2000prm.googlegroups.com...
On Nov 14, 5:40 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
> In the broad scheme of things, Run Time fees are neither here nor there.
>
They actually still are there.  I know of some vertical market package
vendors who migrated to a PC COBOL product to quickly jump from a very
old platform to a newer Unix/Linux platform.  They even added some GUI
and Web type interfaces to it.  All in all, it turned out pretty
nice.  That said, at least one of the companies is bleeding badly
right now.  They have had to roll the run-time licensing fees into
their annual support contract.  While the initial system is priced on
par with their competitors, when a five-year cost of ownership layed
out, they lose.  The other vendors went with native compilers and
don't have to roll in the additional run-time fees as part of their
support contract.

[Pete]

Run Time fees are a choice. I do not suggest they are a good choice, and 
they certainly are not MY choice :-), but in the broad scheme of revenue 
generation for MF I suspect they are not as important as some might think.

> Both companies have realised that COBOL is in decline. Despite talking it
> up, nobody is really fooled into thinking they can continue COBOL
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I would like the opportunity to disagree with this one.  Yes, there
are a lot of companies hiring object programmers and yes, I do object
programming, but a lot of those "new tool" projects are failing.  I'm
talking the kind of spectacular failure which used to only surface
once every couple of years now happening repeatedly at the same
companies.

[Pete]

I'm very happy to respond to your disagreement :-)

The first comment I would make is about tools. The more powerful they are, 
the more damage they can cause in unskilled hands. If you let your 6 year 
old play with your power tools, there's not much point in crying over the 
holes in the furniture. Put the same tools into the hands of a cabinet 
maker, and you can take pride in your new dining table...

You didn't mention what language or tools you are using for "object 
programming", or what the company has failed with.

Certainly, if OO programming is thrown onto COBOL people without proper 
training and orientation, and if the same old waterfall approach is adopted 
with OO as was used with COBOL development, the results are likely to be 
disastrous. Object Orientation requires a shift in concept as well as 
technology, to be successful.

The Project Management approach to OOP needs to be completely different from 
that used with procedural development. (I found this out by experience...) 
If the OOP is managed properly it is very successful (or, it has been every 
time I have used it...Nowadays, I would need a special compelling reason to 
depart from using it - maybe batch development... difficult for me to 
envision...)  People used to managing COBOL development, for the most 
part... (I can thinkof one particular person who was astute and insightful 
enough to realise a change in methodology was warranted), simply don't get 
it.

Training and concomitant change in development approaches are essential 
ingredients for successful OO system development. (As not everyone embraces 
change, this is one of the reasons why OO COBOL was resisted by the COBOL 
community.)

Leaving aside the arguments for training and management adjustment, I stand 
by my statement above. The future is in objects, and, to a lesser extent in 
layered/tiered systems. (This has more to do with the granularity of systems 
and reduces the impact of system software changes on the applications. 
Separation layers, at least at the three basic levels of Presentation, 
Business Logic, and Data Access, are well worth the small extra overhead 
they incur.) When properly implemented, component based systems (based 
around objects) offer HUGE benefits that simply can't be ignored. The 
ability to encapsulate functionality and re-use it anywhere you need it, 
plugging systems together like building with Lego blocks, cuts development 
time dramatically, reduces maintenance and regression testing to almost 
non-existence (in my systems it achieves non-existence, but not everyone 
will develop the way I do...), and eliminates unit debugging. The code is 
smaller, tighter, and more powerful; this makes it easier to extend or 
enhance. Also, as time goes by and the library of useful components grows, 
systems get built even faster. There is a synergy to the whole business. 
Components can be built in any language that is appropriate and supports OOP 
(including COBOL). The difference is that the OO approach in COBOL is not 
the same as the non-OO approach in COBOL. Sometimes this gets overlooked.

The fact that some sites experience disasters with Object based systems, 
does not deter me or change my mind in any way. Until it happens to me, (and 
I have a successful track record of around a dozen small to medium (up to 
$15,000,000) OO projects successfully implemented) I will continue using 
objects/components.

[/Pete]

Would I agree that "indexed file" COBOL is going away?  Mostly.  COBOL
is moving from the foreground to the background.  When companies
implement SOA correctly, almost all of the COBOL still keeps running.
Now it just communicates through port services or MQ Series.

[Pete]

Indexed files are really irrelevant. It simply doesn't matter how the 
corporate repository is organised if the only people who have access to it 
are the IT department.

Yes, SOA is an important technology. But, in the long term it simply buys 
time for COBOL code, it does not guarantee a future for it. Wrapping COBOL 
functions into services is a very good and useful thing to do, but 
eventually these services will be replaced by newer, enhanced ones that will 
be written in newer languages.

[/Pete]

There currently are some very disturbing trends in the industry.
1)  Choosing tools for projects based upon what the $10/day labor
market knows
2)  Hiding failed projects in the financial filings
3)  Directly connecting Web applications to databases which have
sensitive (privacy lawsuit) type data

The first two are really related and have lead to some companies being
audited and delisted.  The third one has very few books written about
what a bad idea it is.  There are a great many books written about HOW
to do it, but apparently the authors aren't technically savy enough to
know it should never be done.

[Pete]

1. Programmers should insist on proper tools. Management should recognise 
that tools are an investment in productivity. Happy teams using their  tools 
of choice, are productive teams. Lobby hard for the tools you need.

2. Bit silly (not to mention, probably illegal...) really. The best action 
for a failed project is to be open about it, take responsibility for it, and 
analyse what went wrong and where exactly it went off the tracks. It isn't 
about blaming people, it is about ensuring that lessons are learned. If 
there was proper financial management in the first place, there would not be 
significant loss, even on "failed" projects.

3. "never" be done?  That seems too strong to me. I see no reason why a Web 
application should be any different to any other application in this regard. 
Provided the proper checks and balances are put in place there is no reason 
why a Web application shouldn't access a sensitive database, any more than a 
destop application.

If your concern is about security, modern Web applications with compiled 
code running on secure servers ("code behind") are far less prone to 
penetration than the good old days when everything was in HTML and 
JavaScript. The fact is that ANYTHING can be cracked, as long as there is a 
legitimate access pathway to it (and if there isn't a legitimate access 
pathway to it you might as well put it in a safe and dump it in the deepest 
part of the Ocean...); it all comes down to relative risk. You may consider 
the Web in general as more risky than your desktop (many people do...) but 
this is not necessarily the case. Much of this perception arises from 
journalists reporting horror stories. While these stories are usually true 
(at least, underneath the journalistic spin...) they are not necessarily 
about things that are likely to happen with well designed, modern web 
development. The fact that HTML is so easy means that anyone who can read 
and write can create a web page. BUT, not all web pages are equal... I know 
of sites that take hundreds of thousands of hits a day, against sensitive 
databases, without problem. (My bank is one... :-))

[/Pete]

just my 0.02

[Pete]
Worth more than that, I think... :-)

Pete.
```

```
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> I would like the opportunity to disagree with this one.  Yes, there
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…

Until the indictments and lawsuits have run their course, you won't
find me mentioning them.

I use many different tools, Java, C++, Qt, and some others.  My
projects don't fail because I don't thrust those tools into an
environment where they are ill-fitted.  Others at my client sites
thrust those tools at everything.  The main reason behind the thrust
is Gartner is payed to market those tools and $10/day off-shore labor
and some companies pay Gartner for really bad advice.  When projects
fail spectacularly and get covered up in the financials, people go to
jail.  Odd that it is never Gartner people going to jail though.

Everybody working on the OO project had their certifications all in a
row for the OO tools they were using.  The projects ALL failed.  Not
one succeeded.  It was really quite astonishing since they spanned
multiple years and you would have thought someone would have shot a
few of them before they hit production.

> The Project Management approach to OOP needs to be completely different from
> that used with procedural development. (I found this out by experience...)
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> it.

This is not entirely true.  In fact, it is mostly false, but a
completely different argument.


>
> Training and concomitant change in development approaches are essential
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Nice marketing line, but has little bearing on the reality of why OO
projects fail and fail big.  The flaw with OOP that isn't readily
admitted by those practicing it fully is a reality faced by most
shops:  Requirements are never complete.  OOP was supposed to make
hacking faster and safer, but it didn't.  When a new or overlooked
requirement hits an OO project and inflicts serious changes to one or
more of the core objects from which others were derived, the project
ends up being a re-write.  I've seen it happen to projects at client
sites, and thanked the programming gods I wasn't working on that
career ending project.

Today's Gartner spouted SDLC process creates "professional project
managers".  This guarantees failure before the project has its first
meeting.  "Professional Project Managers" tend to all have the same
set of flaws:
1)  They've never programmed a production system themselves
2)  They've never worked for any department being affected by the
project so have no concept what-so-ever of how the business works in
that area
3)  They are better at filling in MS Word Templates than they are
about asking the questions which really need to be asked.

I've been watching this happen at one client site since SOX compliance
became a big thing.  They now have a pool of "professional project
managers" getting assigned projects.  In that pool is one project
manager that used to be a developer.  The other dozen or so all went
to business school.  All of them were sent through long and expensive
training courses to become "professional project managers".  Only one
of them brings in successful projects.  All of the others end up
"redefining success" or putting the project on hold once the first
version hits production.

While it is not fair to blame OOP for these failures, OOP is the only
toolset those managers use.  They typically end up getting an off-
shore team which knows even less about the business than the manager.
At that point, the outcome is pre-determined.


OO COBOL made no sense btw.



> Leaving aside the arguments for training and management adjustment, I stand
> by my statement above. The future is in objects, and, to a lesser extent in
> layered/tiered systems. (This has more to do with the granularity of systems

The future is in tiered services which are language/tool independent.
Objects really came and went.  Just how big are the sales of Object
Databases now after being in the market for 10+ years?  Objects have
undeniable use for GUI and Web type front ends, but they are an
undeniably bad choice for the back end of an N-tier system.


>
> [Pete]
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Oh my!  You've never walked into a client site with an order entry
system using indexed files.  One that has been in place for over 10
years and now has a separate indexed file for each prior year, but
that indexed file only contains the paid orders, the unpaid are still
in the active production file.  Why are the other years in separate
files?  Because the indexed file came close to exceeding the maximum
indexed file capacity.

> application should be any different to any other application in this regard.
> Provided the proper checks and balances are put in place there is no reason
> why a Web application shouldn't access a sensitive database, any more than a
> destop application.

Never hook a Web page directly up to a database containing _any_ table
which has privacy issues.  Doing such puts you in the company of TJ
Max and that major book retailer that recently had to notify its
customers of a breach.  Any time you connect such outside access
directly to a database, the database MUST ONLY contain completely
public information.  If it doesn't, you are one new exploit technique
away from being on CNN.

>

Please tell me your bank so I can move any money I have there out and

problem and taking those hits without knowing you have a problem.  The
book retailer found out when federal officials contacted them.
```

```
A very sad picture.

I don't think there is anything I can offer that is likely to change your 
mind, so I'll confine myself to one point only:

You disagreed that a different approach is required for managing OO 
projects, but then you said the OO projects suffer when changes are 
required.

The keys to success with OO are a more agile approach with iteration and 
interaction. This means evolvng a solution and that means extending objects 
and methods as a matter of course,  not throwing your hands up and rewriting 
because a core class changed. It also indicates to me that proper object 
modelling was not carried out in the first place.

There is enough in your post below to persuade me that much is wrong on this 
site, but there is also enough to persuade me that little is to be gained by 
arguing it here.:-)

Thanks for the post. I am genuinely sorry you are having such a bleak 
experience and that it has coloured your opinion about objects and 
components.

I have made this a top post because yours is another mailer that doesn't 
chevron quoted source and it is pretty tiresome to go through and do it by 
hand.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-d43ffd0780"></a>
## Thread: You can teach an old dog new tricks — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Tutorials, books, learning`](topics.md#learning)_

### You can teach an old dog new tricks

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-18T08:44:42+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6odvtbF35rd1U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Micro Focus are doing very well with extending the life of COBOL.

http://www.eweek.com/index2.php?option=content&task=view&id=50422&pop=1&hide_ads=1&page=0&hide_js=1

Riding on the success of their recent PDC, there is a new spring in the step 
of many COBOL developers.

Taking COBOL into .NET and cloud computing is a move that reflects their 
close association with Microsoft, and MS are probably starting to realise 
that there is a lot of benefit for them if people can be moved off  IBM 
hardware into MS supported platforms.

I liked the referenced article because it recognises that COBOL is a lost 
paradigm, but is pragmatic enough to realise there is still scope for COBOL 
code to run in a more cost effective environment. Huge savings can be made 
by moving back end services into cloud computing and Micro Focus should be 
congratulated for facilitating this.

I see this as "buying time" for COBOL sites. I hope that time is used to 
re-invest in training for their people. As mainframers come to grips with 
the .NET and cloud environments there should be a ground swell towards more 
modern approaches.  Back end services running in the cloud on a pay as you 
go basis could be very attractive to many companies.

I'd rather see COBOL running in the cloud for batch services, than Ruby... 
:-)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: You can teach an old dog new tricks

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2008-11-17T18:31:00-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<80oUk.21444$us4.13130@newsfe14.iad>`
- **References:** `<6odvtbF35rd1U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
There are a couple of references in the Oct 27/08 issue of Eweek casting
doubt on the future of cloud computing - p10, part of the "Up-Front" op-ed
"A high-level view of IT and the economy"; p42, "Concerns over the cloud";
and Specner Katt's column.  (I don't get the e-mail version so I can't give
you URL's).  To be fair, the consensus seems to be that cloud computing will
work for SMB's but not the big guys, but the point is that unalloyed
enthusiasm is not present.

Representing the Cassandra Institute:

PL


Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: You can teach an old dog new tricks

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-11-18T14:20:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6oejjrF38skfU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<6odvtbF35rd1U1@mid.individual.net> <80oUk.21444$us4.13130@newsfe14.iad>`

```


"tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote in message 
news:80oUk.21444$us4.13130@newsfe14.iad...
> There are a couple of references in the Oct 27/08 issue of Eweek casting
> doubt on the future of cloud computing - p10, part of the "Up-Front" op-ed
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Representing the Cassandra Institute:

:-) Thanks Peter. Let's not forget that Cassandra was right... :-)

I agree that the future of it is by no means certain. My "contacts" tell me 
there is much excitement in Microsoft about it and I understand IBM are 
planning an equivalent of their own.

For SMBs it certainly makes sense. If a business is moving to an SOA 
approach anyway, then cloud computing can make life a lot easier (and 
cheaper). For larger sites it may not be such an attractive option. 
Sensitive sites will have concerns about security; it will be some time yet 
before businesses will agree to let someone else store their data in a plce 
where they don't have physical access to it...

As always, time will tell.

Nevertheless, I saw this article as being positive for COBOL.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-500e765497"></a>
## Thread: FILE-AID (or similar) for Windows — 1 this month / 2 total

### FILE-AID (or similar) for Windows

- **From:** na <google@dcbarry.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-18T09:17:59-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0b957558-664e-4cf8-af1e-4766ab3428e4@c22g2000prc.googlegroups.com>`

```
Apologies in advance if this could be posted somewhere better... open
to suggestions.

I haven't lived in the mainframe world for almost 20 years, so I've
forgotten almost everything from that era.  However, I do recall that
there was a "FILE-AID/PC" product that allowed working locally with
datasets and runbooks, etc.

From what I can see on the Compuware site, its no longer supported,
and it looks like there is no direct replacement (for Windows).  They
seem to have tools with much more complexity (FILE-AID/CS) , but the
bottom line is I just need something simple that can (on Windows) take
in a copybook and a dataset and give me something readable, and it
would be nice to be able to reduce a dataset by specifying a match on
a given field.  Right now we are trying to use Microsoft Log Parser,
but that requires manually converting the copybooks.

I've tried googling without much luck.  I could also use something
that could convert a copybook to SQL layout.


Any suggestions?

Thanks to all!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MF Cobol ACCEPT with TIME-OUT

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2008-11-19T11:29:31-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gg1gof118k9@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<ZQFUk.7421$yr3.1146@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<TdDUk.5924$W06.1937@flpi148.ffdc.sbc.com> <gfuvj8$hhf$1@reader1.panix.com> <VpDUk.8956$ZP4.4715@nlpi067.nbdc.sbc.com> <gfv3jq$16d$1@reader1.panix.com> <ZQFUk.7421$yr3.1146@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```
C C wrote:
> 
> It was my mistake.  I had the following code which did not give me a compile 
> error:
>         ACCEPT TEST-SCREEN DISPLAY TEST-SCREEN TIMEOUT AFTER 180.

DISPLAY starts a new statement, so the TIMEOUT phrase would not apply
to the ACCEPT.

That looks like it ought to have failed to compile, since there's no
TIMEOUT phrase defined for DISPLAY. (Though there's no good reason why
not; DISPLAY can time out, of course, if the output device is not
local.) So that looks like a bug, albeit a minor one.

You don't say what MF compiler you're using, but I just tried this
with Net Express 5.1, and the compiler does indeed allow a TIMEOUT
phrase on DISPLAY. It changes the behavior of a simple DISPLAY from
ANSI to ADIS (as eg "AT LINE 1" would).
```

#### ↳ Re: MF Cobol ACCEPT with TIME-OUT

